Question 1
Skipped
You notice that some Cortex function calls are taking longer than 30 seconds, causing timeouts in your application. The same prompts sometimes run quickly. What is the most likely cause and solution?
Correct answer
A. The model is experiencing variable load; implement retry logic with exponential backoff.
B. The warehouse is too small; increase the warehouse size for Cortex processing.
C. The prompts are too long; implement prompt chunking to reduce token count.
D. There's network congestion between regions; use a warehouse in a closer region.
E. The max_tokens parameter is set too high; reduce it to decrease generation time.
Overall explanation
Most likely cause and solution: - Likely cause: A. The model is experiencing variable load; implement retry logic with exponential backoff. - Rationale: Inference latency can vary with server load. When traffic spikes, some requests may timeout or take longer than expected. Implementing retry logic with exponential backoff helps tolerate temporary delays and avoids hammering the service. Detailed proposition-by-proposition analysis: A. The model is experiencing variable load; implement retry logic with exponential backoff - Why this fits: Cloud-based model hosting often shows fluctuating latency depending on current demand. Retries with backoff smooth out spikes and reduce the chance of timeouts from transient delays. - Pros: Improves reliability under load, simple to implement, widely recommended for remote API calls. - Cons: If persistent high load, retries may still fail or incur higher costs/time; must cap retries and implement jitter to avoid synchronized retries. - When to use: If timeouts occur sporadically and you notice latency variance rather than a constant long tail. B. The warehouse is too small; increase the warehouse size for Cortex processing - Why not primary: “Warehouse size” typically relates to data processing capacity in some analytics contexts. If Cortex here refers to a cloud inference service, this option is not a standard or likely cause for per-call latency timeouts in real-time model inference. - Pros if applicable: In some platforms, more compute could reduce latency. - Cons: Unclear mapping to Cortex inference; not a general or reliable fix for API-timeouts during prompt generation. - When to use: Only if your platform explicitly documents per-request latency tied to a configurable processing warehouse and you can justify scaling there. C. The prompts are too long; implement prompt chunking to reduce token count - Why this helps sometimes: Longer prompts can increase tokenization, processing time, and memory usage, potentially slowing responses. - Pros: Can reduce per-request compute and latency; helps if you’re hitting token or context length limits. - Cons: For many models, prompt chunking changes the result and can degrade quality; not always a fix for network/API timeouts caused by remote serving variability. - When to use: If you consistently approach token limits or observe longer generation times tied to very large prompts. D. There's network congestion between regions; use a warehouse in a closer region - Why this helps: If you’re routing requests across regions, additional network latency can contribute to timeouts. - Pros: Reducing network distance generally lowers latency. - Cons: This depends on your deployment: Cortex/hosting region availability and your network topology. Not always configurable as a “warehouse” option. - When to use: If you have multi-region deployments and can observe regional latency spikes or inconsistent performance. E. The max_tokens parameter is set too high; reduce it to decrease generation time - Why this helps: Larger max_tokens can lengthen generation time, since the model must produce more tokens before completing a response. - Pros: Directly reduces potential generation time; simple adjustment. - Cons: Limits output length; may cut off needed information if set too low. - When to use: If you notice latency correlating with lengthy generations or you’re constrained on response size. Recommendation: - Primary: A. The model is experiencing variable load; implement retry logic with exponential backoff. - Secondary checks (if issues persist): investigate D (region/network latency) and E (generation time related to max_tokens). Consider C if prompt length is unusually large and you’re hitting performance or token-length constraints. Final answer: A is the most likely cause; implement retry logic with exponential backoff.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Latency, Reliability, and API retry/backoff
Question 2
Skipped
When implementing semantic search across multiple languages, what is the most important factor for ensuring consistent performance across different languages?
A. Using the same embedding model for all languages.
Correct answer
B. Ensuring the embedding model was trained on multilingual data.
C. Translating all text to English before generating embeddings.
D. Using language-specific embedding models for each language.
E. Increasing the vector dimensions for non-English languages.
Overall explanation
1. A. Using the same embedding model for all languages - Pros: If the model is truly multilingual, this can help align representations across languages. - Cons: Simply reusing a single model without ensuring it is trained on multilingual data may fail to produce comparable embeddings for different languages. The key is whether the model can map semantics cross-linguistically, not just the sameness of the model. 2. B. Ensuring the embedding model was trained on multilingual data - Correct. A multilingual training corpus (and architecture) enables cross-language semantic alignment, so similar concepts have similar vector representations across languages. This underpins consistent performance in multilingual semantic search. 3. C. Translating all text to English before generating embeddings - Cons: Translation can introduce errors, alter meaning, and add latency. It also prevents native-language nuances from being captured. Relying on translation often degrades performance and consistency across languages. 4. D. Using language-specific embedding models for each language - Pros: Good within-language understanding, as each model can be tuned to its language. - Cons: Cross-language retrieval requires mapping between languages; separate models may produce poorly aligned embeddings across languages, harming consistency in multilingual search results. 5. E. Increasing the vector dimensions for non-English languages - Pros: Higher capacity could capture more nuances. - Cons: Dimensionality alone does not guarantee cross-language alignment. Without multilingual training and proper alignment, this can lead to imbalance, overfitting, or inefficiency without improving cross-language consistency. Correct proposition: B. Ensuring the embedding model was trained on multilingual data Detailed rationale: - Cross-language consistency hinges on the embedding space being aligned across languages. Multilingual training teaches the model to map semantically related concepts from different languages to nearby vectors, enabling reliable cross-language retrieval. - A single model (A) helps only if it’s truly multilingual; otherwise, it’s not guaranteed. - Translation (C) introduces risk and latency, and may degrade alignment. - Language-specific models (D) can perform well within each language but often struggle to align across languages. - Increasing dimensions (E) without multilingual alignment doesn’t address the core issue.
Domain
Knowledge area: Multilingual semantic search / cross-languag
Question 3
Skipped
A financial institution needs to ensure that all Cortex-generated SQL queries from Cortex Analyst are compliant with their internal auditing standards before execution. What is the most secure implementation?
A. Use the VQR to only allow pre-approved query patterns.
B. Implement a manual approval workflow where generated SQL is reviewed by an analyst before execution.
Correct answer
C. Use a stored procedure that validates the generated SQL against a set of security rules.
D. Configure Snowflake to require secondary approval for all queries from Cortex Analyst.
E. Limit Cortex Analyst to read-only views with no sensitive data.
Overall explanation
Here’s a detailed proposition-by-proposition analysis for securing Cortex-generated SQL queries. 1) A. Use the VQR to only allow pre-approved query patterns - What it implies: A validation rule set (VQR) that whitelists specific, pre-approved SQL patterns. Cortex Analyst can generate only queries that conform to these patterns; anything outside is rejected. - Pros: - Strong enforcement of known-safe patterns. - Low runtime overhead after rules are in place. - Reduces the surface area for unexpected or risky SQL constructs. - Cons: - Requires comprehensive, up-to-date rule maintenance; new legitimate queries may be blocked until rules are updated. - May be brittle if Cortex generates variations not anticipated by the rules. - Does not inherently handle context-sensitive checks (e.g., whether data access is compliant with a given role). - Why it’s valuable: It provides automated, centralized control over the allowed syntax and structure, reducing the chance of unsafe queries slipping through. However, its rigidity can hinder agility and may miss context checks. 2) B. Implement a manual approval workflow where generated SQL is reviewed by an analyst before execution - What it implies: Every Cortex-generated query is held for human review and approval prior to running. - Pros: - Human oversight can catch nuanced risks, policy violations, and data-access issues not captured by automated rules. - Clear audit trail of who approved what and when. - Cons: - Operational bottleneck: delays can slow legitimate data work. - Dependency on availability and diligence of analysts; risk of human error or inconsistent approvals. - Not scalable for high-volume query generation. - Why it’s valuable: It strengthens governance and compliance through explicit approvals, but at the cost of speed and scalability. It’s not “most secure” in an automated, enforceable sense because it relies on human processes. 3) C. Use a stored procedure that validates the generated SQL against a set of security rules - What it implies: The SQL produced by Cortex Analyst is funneled through a stored procedure that enforces security constraints (e.g., access controls, data masking rules, forbidden constructs, permitted schemas/tables, row/column-level access, etc.) before execution. - Pros: - Centralized, automated enforcement at the database layer. - Consistent application of security policy regardless of who generates the query. - Can be designed to incorporate context (e.g., user role, data sensitivity, purpose) and apply dynamic checks. - Reduces reliance on external tooling; immediate, enforceable guardrails. - Cons: - Requires careful, comprehensive definition of security rules and edge cases. - Needs maintenance as data governance needs evolve (updated rules, new tables/columns, policy changes). - May introduce latency if checks are complex. - Why it’s the strongest choice: It enforces compliance at the point of execution with automated, auditable checks. It reduces risk of non-compliant queries slipping through and scales with volume, making it the most robust “secure implementation” among automated options. 4) D. Configure Snowflake to require secondary approval for all queries from Cortex Analyst - What it implies: A database-level policy in Snowflake that all Cortex Analyst-originated queries must pass a secondary approval step before they run. - Pros: - Enforces governance at the data platform level with an auditable approval trail. - Can be combined with separate identity/permissioning to ensure approvals come from authorized individuals. - Cons: - Similar to B in workflow drawback unless integrated with automation; introduces latency and potential bottlenecks. - The “secondary approval” might still be bypassed if the initial query is pre-approved or if automation is misconfigured. - Implementation complexity depends on Snowflake features (e.g., tasks, pipelines, access control) and may require orchestration outside SQL. - Why it’s valuable: It provides strong governance, but its effectiveness depends on how the approval flow is implemented and integrated. It’s more of an organizational control than a pure automated enforcement mechanism. 5) E. Limit Cortex Analyst to read-only views with no sensitive data - What it implies: Users can only query predefined read-only views that expose non-sensitive data. No direct access to base tables or sensitive columns. - Pros: - Minimizes data exposure by design. - Reduces risk of accidentally running harmful queries or accessing restricted data. - Simple, environment-wide access control with clear data segmentation. - Cons: - Limits the usefulness of Cortex Analyst if there’s a need to access broader data (even in a compliant form). - Requires continuous maintenance to ensure views cover legitimate use cases and do not expose sensitive data indirectly. - Doesn’t address the risk of complex queries that could cause performance or policy violations through views themselves (e.g., data masking rules must be enforced in views). - Why it’s valuable: It greatly reduces data risk by restricting data surface, but it does not guarantee compliance of the queries themselves or prevent unsafe patterns within allowed views. Most secure implementation - The most secure, automated enforcement is option C: Use a stored procedure that validates the generated SQL against a set of security rules. It provides centralized, consistent, auditable, and scalable enforcement at execution time, reducing reliance on manual steps or ad-hoc pattern whitelisting. - Options A and D add valuable governance layers but depend on rule maintenance (A) or workflow orchestration (D) and may not cover all risk vectors as comprehensively as automated, rule-based validation at the execution point. - Option E reduces data exposure but does not guarantee that queries, once validated as safe, are compliant with all auditing standards. - Option B offers strong governance via human oversight but is slower and less scalable than automated validation. Conclusion - Best choice: C. Use a stored procedure that validates the generated SQL against a set of security rules. It delivers automated, consistent, and auditable enforcement aligned with secure development and data governance practices.
Domain
Knowledge area: Data security and governance (data access co
Question 4
Skipped
You're building a recommendation system that uses both collaborative filtering (user-item interactions) and content-based filtering (vector embeddings). What is the key challenge in combining these approaches?
A. Converting user interactions into vector embeddings.
Correct answer
B. Normalizing the different score ranges from each recommendation method.
C. Handling the cold start problem for new users and items.
D. Ensuring real-time updates to both recommendation engines.
E. Managing the storage requirements for both approaches.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Converting user interactions into vector embeddings** - This is a common step in content-based and hybrid systems: turning interactions (ratings, clicks) into vector representations. While it can be technically challenging (choosing features, embedding models, dimensionality), it is not the fundamental hurdle of combining collaborative filtering with content-based filtering. The conversion itself is a design choice and can be managed with standard embedding techniques. 2. **B. Normalizing the different score ranges from each recommendation method** - This is the key challenge. Collaborative filtering outputs similarity or ranking scores that may live on one scale (e.g., 0–1 or raw hit counts), while content-based signals (embeddings) often produce cosine similarities, dot products, or distance-based scores on different scales. When fusing these signals, you must normalize or calibrate them so that one source doesn’t dominate simply due to scale. This entails: - Aligning score ranges across methods. - Calibrating confidence and ranking signals. - Designing a fusion mechanism (weighted blend, stacking, or learning-to-rank) that respects the disparate natures of the inputs. If normalization is mishandled, the hybrid system may over-rely on one signal or produce poor recommendations. 3. **C. Handling the cold start problem for new users and items** - Cold start is a known challenge for both CF and content-based methods, but it is not unique to combining them. Hybrid approaches aim to mitigate it (e.g., using content features for new items/users), but the question asks for the key challenge in combining the approaches. Cold start is important, yet the central operational difficulty in a hybrid fusion is how to merge heterogeneous signals rather than the existence of cold-start issues per se. 4. **D. Ensuring real-time updates to both recommendation engines** - Real-time updates can be challenging in production, especially when both CF models and embedding-based models require retraining or recomputation. However, this is more about engineering complexity and latency, not the core conceptual challenge of combining the methods. It can be addressed with incremental updates, caching, and asynchronous pipelines. 5. **E. Managing the storage requirements for both approaches** - Storage concerns exist (storing interaction histories, embeddings, indices, etc.), but they are typically secondary to the modeling and fusion challenges. With proper data management, hardware, and compression, storage is manageable and not the primary barrier to effective hybrid recommendations. Correct Proposition **B. Normalizing the different score ranges from each recommendation method** Detailed Explanation - Collaboration vs. content signals: CF outputs similarity or ranking based on user-item interactions, while content-based signals rely on vector embeddings and similarity measures. Their numeric ranges, distributions, and interpretation differ. - Fusion implications: When combining signals (e.g., via weighted averaging, learning-to-rank, or meta-models), misaligned scales can bias the result toward one source. - Practical steps: - Normalize scores (e.g., min-max, z-score) or calibrate confidences for each source. - Use a fusion strategy that accounts for reliability and context (e.g., per-item or per-user weighting, or a learned model that takes both scores as features). - Validate with A/B tests to ensure the hybrid system improves metrics without unintended dominance by a single signal. - Why others are less central: - A is a design task but not the fundamental hurdle of hybridization. - C is a known issue but is addressed by the hybrid approach; it’s not the core integration challenge. - D and E are engineering concerns, not the core methodological challenge of merging CF and content-based signals.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Recommender Systems — Hybrid approaches (CF
Question 5
Skipped
An organization wants to use fine-tuned Cortex models but is concerned about the potential for data leakage between different fine-tuning jobs. What is the primary security measure to prevent this?
A. Use separate Snowflake accounts for each fine-tuning project.
Correct answer
B. Ensure proper data isolation in the training data preparation process.
C. Use different warehouse sizes for each fine-tuning job.
D. Implement network segmentation between fine-tuning environments.
E. Use encryption with different keys for each fine-tuning dataset.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Use separate Snowflake accounts for each fine-tuning project.** - Pros: Strong isolation at the account level; limits cross-project access. - Cons: Adds operational overhead (provisioning/manage multiple accounts) and may not address all in-data leakage risks during preprocessing or model training if data is re-used within accounts. 2. **B. Ensure proper data isolation in the training data preparation process.** - Pros: Directly prevents leakage by ensuring that data from one fine-tuning project cannot be mixed, merged, or inadvertently exposed to another project during ingestion, preprocessing, and dataset splitting. This is the primary design-time control for preventing data cross-contamination in the training pipeline. - Cons: Requires meticulous data handling, access controls, and validated pipelines; may still need additional controls (e.g., separate environments) for defense in depth. 3. **C. Use different warehouse sizes for each fine-tuning job.** - Pros: Resource isolation (performance) but not data isolation. - Cons: Does not prevent data leakage; purely affects compute resources and may not impact security of data contents. 4. **D. Implement network segmentation between fine-tuning environments.** - Pros: Network-level isolation helps limit lateral movement and external access. - Cons: Primarily addresses access control and network exposure rather than ensuring training data isolation; insufficient alone to prevent data leakage in the data handling pipeline. 5. **E. Use encryption with different keys for each fine-tuning dataset.** - Pros: Protects data at rest and enforces per-dataset confidentiality. - Cons: Encryption alone does not prevent leakage if data handling or preprocessing steps reuse or expose data across jobs; needs correct key management and access policies to be effective. Correct Proposition **B. Ensure proper data isolation in the training data preparation process.** Detailed Explanation - Data leakage in fine-tuning arises when datasets from different projects can unintentionally mix, be exposed, or be reused across jobs during ingestion, preprocessing, or splitting. The most reliable primary control is to enforce strict data isolation in the training data preparation pipeline: - Use separate, validated data pipelines for each fine-tuning project. - Enforce strict access controls so only the intended data is loaded for a given job. - Implement checks that prevent cross-dataset joins, merges, or transfers between projects during preprocessing. - Maintain audit trails and data labeling to ensure provenance is preserved and leakage is detectable. - While other options offer valuable security or operational benefits (account-level isolation, resource segmentation, network controls, encryption), they do not directly guarantee that training data from different projects cannot leak into another project’s fine-tuning process. Data isolation in the preparation stage directly addresses the core risk. Conclusion To prevent data leakage between different fine-tuning jobs, the primary security measure is to ensure proper data isolation in the training data preparation process (Option B).
Domain
ML Security & Data Privacy — Data isolation in training data
Question 6
Skipped
A company is using Document AI to process a large volume of scanned legal contracts. While typed clauses are extracted with high accuracy, they notice significant variability in the extraction of handwritten witness signatures and dates. The handwritten content is generally legible. What is the most strategic approach to ensure reliable data capture from these mixed-content documents for downstream processing?
A. Pre-process all documents using a separate, specialized third-party handwriting recognition service before submitting them to Document AI.
B. Configure Document AI to ignore all handwritten sections by applying a custom model trained only on typed text structures.
Correct answer
C. Use Document AI's standard processing for all content, but implement a validation workflow that routes documents with low-confidence extractions for specific handwritten fields to a human review queue.
D. Increase the resolution and quality of all document scans to the maximum possible, assuming this will resolve the handwriting recognition inaccuracy.
E. Replace Document AI with a series of traditional SQL string functions and regular expressions programmed to identify handwritten date and signature patterns.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Pre-process all documents using a separate, specialized third-party handwriting recognition service before submitting them to Document AI. - Pros: Could improve handwriting recognition accuracy by using a dedicated handwriting model. - Cons: Adds another system, integration complexity, latency, and potential data handling/security concerns. Handwritten content varies widely; a separate service may not align with your downstream data schemas or be cost-inefficient. It also risks inconsistencies between the handwriting outputs and the typed-text expectations of downstream processors. 2. B. Configure Document AI to ignore all handwritten sections by applying a custom model trained only on typed text structures. - Pros: Simplifies downstream processing by removing handwritten content. - Cons: Not feasible if handwritten fields (like dates and signatures) are required for business or legal workflows. Ignoring them would result in loss of critical data. This approach compromises data completeness and undermines downstream processing requirements. 3. C. Use Document AI's standard processing for all content, but implement a validation workflow that routes documents with low-confidence extractions for specific handwritten fields to a human review queue. - Pros: Leverages existing, mature OCR/ML capabilities for the bulk of the data while explicitly handling uncertainty in handwritten fields. The human-in-the-loop can verify and correct dates and signatures, improving overall accuracy without abandoning automation. Enables scalable, cost-efficient processing by only escalating a subset of documents. - Cons: Requires a well-designed validation workflow, service levels for human review, and integration with the queue system. There is some overhead to manage, but it is a standard, effective pattern for mixed-content scenarios. 4. D. Increase the resolution and quality of all document scans to the maximum possible, assuming this will resolve the handwriting recognition inaccuracy. - Pros: Higher quality images can help OCR/handwriting models perform better. - Cons: Scanning more than necessary can increase storage, processing time, and costs. Even with higher resolution, handwriting recognition may still require interpretive models or human validation. This approach also delays processing and is not a comprehensive solution for mixed content. 5. E. Replace Document AI with a series of traditional SQL string functions and regular expressions programmed to identify handwritten date and signature patterns. - Pros: Very lightweight for structured, highly deterministic patterns. - Cons: Handwritten content is not reliably captured by regex in raw images or OCR outputs; this approach would require substantial OCR output preprocessing and is brittle. It generally cannot handle variability in handwriting, formats, or placement, and would likely perform poorly on signed dates and witness signatures across documents. Correct Proposition The most strategic approach is: C. Use Document AI's standard processing for all content, but implement a validation workflow that routes documents with low-confidence extractions for specific handwritten fields to a human review queue. Detailed Explanation 1. Mixed-content reality: - Typed text is usually highly accurate with Document AI, while handwritten sections (even legible) introduce variability that standard models struggle with consistently. - Critical fields like dates and signatures often require high accuracy for downstream workflows (compliance, audit trails). 2. Workflow design benefits: - Leverages the strengths of Document AI for the bulk of the content (typed text) while acknowledging and mitigating uncertainty where handwriting is involved. - A human-in-the-loop for low-confidence handwritten fields ensures data integrity without sacrificing automation for the majority of the document. 3. Confidence-based routing: - Implement thresholds (confidence scores) for specific handwritten fields. - If a field’s confidence falls below the threshold, route to a dedicated review queue where trained staff can validate or correct the value before downstream processing or storage. 4. Efficiency and cost considerations: - Only a subset of documents requires human review, making the process scalable and cost-effective. - Automation handles high-volume, typed content, while exceptions are managed with quality control, reducing risk of errors in critical data. 5. Data quality and downstream processing: - Corrected handwritten data can be fed into downstream systems with high reliability. - The approach supports auditability, traceability, and better compliance since uncertainties are explicitly reviewed and logged. 6. Practical implementation notes: - Define clear SLAs for review queues to avoid bottlenecks. - Maintain a feedback loop to improve model performance over time (e.g., using corrections to retrain or fine-tune handwriting models). - Ensure secure handling and privacy alignment for any human-in-the-loop data processing. Conclusion Option C provides a balanced, scalable, and reliable strategy for mixed-content documents: it uses Document AI where it performs best and applies human review selectively for handwritten fields that trigger low-confidence, delivering high-quality data for downstream processing without overhauling your entire pipeline.
Domain
Knowledge area: Intelligent Document Processing with Documen
Question 7
Skipped
A data engineering team has successfully fine-tuned a foundation model using SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE on a dataset of 10,000 proprietary technical support tickets. They now want to deploy this model to power a real-time chatbot. However, they are unsure about the correct method to reference their custom model within their application code. Which of the following represents the valid and recommended approach?
A. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('JOB_12345', 'What is the error code?') where JOB_12345 is the fine-tuning job ID.
Correct answer
B. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('finetuned_model', 'What is the error code?') where finetuned_model is the name provided in the OBJECT_CONSTRUCT options during the fine-tuning job.
C. CALL SNOWFLAKE.ML.USE_MODEL('finetuned_model'); followed by a standard COMPLETE call without specifying a model.
D. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('COMPLETE', 'finetuned_model', 'What is the error code?').
E. Create a new UDF that wraps the fine-tuned model logic and call the UDF directly.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('JOB_12345', 'What is the error code?') where JOB_12345 is the fine-tuning job ID. - Why it’s not ideal: After finetuning, you don’t typically pass the original finetuning job ID to a COMPLETE call. Cortex expects you to reference the actual deployed finetuned model. The first argument should identify the model to use, not the job that performed the training. Using the job ID may not resolve to a usable inference model in the serving context. 2. B. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('finetuned_model', 'What is the error code?') where finetuned_model is the name provided in the OBJECT_CONSTRUCT options during the fine-tuning job. - Why this is correct: In Cortex finetuning, you assign a model name (or identifier) when configuring the fine-tune job (often via OBJECT_CONSTRUCT options). The deployed, served model is then referenced by that name in subsequent COMPLETE calls. The proper usage is to pass the model name as the first argument to SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE to query the finetuned model for a response. - Additional nuances: - Ensure the model name you supply matches exactly what was configured at finetune time. - The prompt (second argument) is the user query or instruction you want the model to respond to. - This approach aligns with Snowflake Cortex’s serving semantics, where a named finetuned model is invoked by name. 3. C. CALL SNOWFLAKE.ML.USE_MODEL('finetuned_model'); followed by a standard COMPLETE call without specifying a model. - Why it’s not correct: Snowflake ML/USe_MODEL is not a standard, documented sequence for Cortex model invocation. After selecting a model with USE_MODEL, Cortex typically still requires the COMPLETE call to specify the model context. Moreover, mixing a separate USE_MODEL step with a generic COMPLETE that omits the model is not the canonical pattern described in Cortex finetuning guidance. - Practical issue: If the function exists in a particular release, it’s not the recommended or widely supported approach for invoking a finetuned Cortex model in real-time chat scenarios. 4. D. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('COMPLETE', 'finetuned_model', 'What is the error code?'). - Why it’s not correct: CORTEX.FINETUNE is for training/finetuning operations, not for serving/inferencing. You don’t run a COMPLETE-like operation through FINETUNE after the model has been deployed. This option confuses training-time APIs with inference-time APIs. 5. E. Create a new UDF that wraps the fine-tuned model logic and call the UDF directly. - Why it’s not standard: While you could wrap calls to Cortex in a UDF, the recommended approach for real-time inference is to call SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE (or equivalent serving function) directly with the model name. Creating a custom UDF adds extra maintenance, latency, and potential for misalignment with Cortex’s model lifecycle and auth/permissions. Correct Proposition B. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('finetuned_model', 'What is the error code?') where finetuned_model is the name provided in the OBJECT_CONSTRUCT options during the fine-tuning job. Detailed Explanation - Model reference after finetuning: - During finetuning, you provide a model identifier/name (often via an OBJECT_CONSTRUCT payload). This name becomes the reference you use when invoking the model for inference. - The correct invocation format for real-time inference is SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE(model_name, prompt). - Argument semantics: - First argument: the exact finetuned model name (as configured during fine-tune). This tells Cortex which deployed model to use for the inference session. - Second argument: the user prompt or query you want the model to respond to. - Why A is incorrect: - Passing a finetuning job ID is not the standard or reliable way to cue Cortex to a deployed model. The job ID is a training artifact, not the serving artifact. - Why C is not standard practice: - There is no well-documented, portable, and recommended pattern to switch context with a USE_MODEL step and then use a generic COMPLETE without a model spec. The canonical approach is to specify the model in the COMPLETE call directly. - Why D and E are not appropriate: - D attempts to call a finetune operation during inference, which is not how Cortex serves finetuned models. - E adds an unnecessary UDF layer when Cortex already provides a direct, efficient inference API with proper model scoping. Conclusion Use B: reference the finetuned model by its configured name in the COMPLETE call to obtain responses from the real-time chatbot powered by Snowflake Cortex finetuning.
Domain
Snowflake Cortex finetuning and real-time inference (model r
Question 8
Skipped
A development team is building an application that uses AI_COMPLETE to generate product descriptions. The downstream system requires the output to be a valid JSON object with specific keys: product_name, summary, key_features (as an array), and target_audience. They are currently using a detailed prompt to instruct the model, but sometimes the output is malformed JSON or includes extra text. Which solution guarantees the required JSON structure?
A. Use EXTRACT_ANSWER four separate times, once for each required key, and then manually construct the JSON object in the application code.
B. Use the standard COMPLETE function but append the text "You must return only valid JSON. Do not include any other text or explanations." to the prompt.
Correct answer
C. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by defining and providing the exact JSON schema in the function's options parameter.
D. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize the product, then use a lookup table to fetch a pre-written JSON template for that category.
E. Implement a post-processing step that uses JavaScript UDFs to parse the LLM's text response and extract the JSON using regex.
Overall explanation
1) A. Use EXTRACT_ANSWER four separate times, once for each required key, and then manually construct the JSON object in the application code. - Pros: - You control the final JSON structure in code. - Reduces risk of extraneous text if you strictly parse the four extracted values. - Cons/Limitations: - Requires multiple calls and manual assembly, increasing latency and complexity. - Parsing reliability depends on the consistency of each extraction step. - Still leaves room for formatting quirks when combining into a single JSON object. - Conclusion: Not the most resilient or elegant solution for guaranteeing a single valid JSON response. 2) B. Use the standard COMPLETE function but append the text "You must return only valid JSON. Do not include any other text or explanations." to the prompt. - Pros: - Encourages JSON-only output. - Cons/Limitations: - Not a strict guarantee; LLMs can still produce formatting issues, extra text, or invalid JSON. - If the model slips, downstream parsing can fail. - No formal schema enforcement; still relies on model compliance. - Conclusion: Improves odds but does not guarantee valid JSON structure or schema. 3) C. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by defining and providing the exact JSON schema in the function's options parameter. - Pros: - Explicit schema guidance ensures the model formats output as a JSON object matching the required fields. - Structured outputs typically include metadata like type, required fields, and examples, increasing determinism. - Reduces post-processing: the result is designed to be valid JSON that conforms to the schema. - Cons/Limitations: - Requires support for Structured Outputs in the AI_COMPLETE API and correct usage of the options parameter. - You must ensure the schema is comprehensive (e.g., field names, types, array structures) and that the model adheres to it. - Conclusion: The strongest, most robust option for guaranteeing the required JSON structure. 4) D. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize the product, then use a lookup table to fetch a pre-written JSON template for that category. - Pros: - If categories map to fixed templates, you get consistent JSON structure. - Cons/Limitations: - Requires comprehensive and up-to-date templates for all categories. - Risk of template mismatch with actual product details; may lead to inaccurate content. - Less flexible for products that don’t fit neatly into a category. - Conclusion: Could work in a tightly controlled catalog but brittle and labor-intensive to maintain. 5) E. Implement a post-processing step that uses JavaScript UDFs to parse the LLM's text response and extract the JSON using regex. - Pros: - Keeps the LLM output free-form, with a deterministic extraction step. - Cons/Limitations: - Regex-based extraction is brittle; JSON could be malformed in corner cases (nested structures, escapes, etc.). - Parsing errors require additional error handling and can fail gracefully only with extra logic. - Conclusion: Not as reliable as using a structured-output mechanism; adds complexity and potential failure points. Correct Proposition C. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by defining and providing the exact JSON schema in the function's options parameter. Detailed Explanation - Why structured outputs help: - The model is guided to produce a result that exactly matches a predefined schema, minimizing free-form text. - This reduces or eliminates the need for heavy post-processing, since the output is designed to be valid JSON. - How to implement: - Define a clear JSON schema in the AI_COMPLETE options (fields: product_name, summary, key_features as an array, target_audience). - Provide example data in the schema or examples section to steer the model toward the correct structure and types. - Enable strict parsing on the downstream system to validate JSON against the schema before using it. - Why not the others: - A relies on multiple extractions and manual assembly, which is error-prone and slower. - B depends on the model following a single instruction but cannot guarantee perfect JSON formatting. - D depends on category templates, which may not cover all products accurately or flexibly. - E introduces brittle post-processing; if the JSON is malformed, regex extraction can fail or miss data. - Result: A deterministic, schema-aligned JSON output that matches the downstream system’s requirements with minimal risk of malformed JSON.
Domain
Prompt engineering for structured outputs (schema-driven gen
Question 9

When designing a context-aware document Q&A system using Cortex functions, which combination of three factors is MOST critical for achieving high-quality, reliable answers from large documents?
A. Using the most expensive/largest available model, setting temperature to 0 for all queries, and using the same prompt template for all document types.
Correct answer
B. Implementing intelligent document chunking strategies, employing few-shot learning in prompts, and configuring task-appropriate temperature settings.
C. Always using the latest model version, processing the entire document in one call regardless of length, and using a high temperature for creative answers.
D. Pre-embedding all documents, using vector similarity for all queries, and avoiding the use of the COMPLETE function altogether.
E. Setting a very high max_tokens value, using a low temperature for summarization tasks, and caching all previous responses.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Using the most expensive/largest available model, setting temperature to 0 for all queries, and using the same prompt template for all document types.** - Not optimal: Bigger models can help, but cost and latency matter. Temperature 0 reduces creativity and may hurt when handling diverse document types and nuanced questions. A uniform prompt template across all document types ignores the diversity in structure and user intent, leading to poorer QA quality. 2. **B. Implementing intelligent document chunking strategies, employing few-shot learning in prompts, and configuring task-appropriate temperature settings.** - Strongly favorable: - Intelligent chunking preserves context and makes large documents tractable. - Few-shot prompts help guide the model to extract relevant answer formats for different tasks. - Task-appropriate temperature balances determinism and creativity per task (e.g., low for factual answers, slightly higher for summaries). - This combination directly addresses core challenges in long-document QA. 3. **C. Always using the latest model version, processing the entire document in one call regardless of length, and using a high temperature for creative answers.** - Not practical: Processing entire long documents in one call is often infeasible due to length limits and latency. A high temperature for general QA reduces reliability and precision, which is undesirable for fact-based Q&A from large documents. 4. **D. Pre-embedding all documents, using vector similarity for all queries, and avoiding the use of the COMPLETE function altogether.** - Partially valid approach (embedding + vector search) but incomplete: - Pre-embedding and vector similarity are powerful for locating relevant sections, but avoiding any COMPLETE-type function can hinder comprehensive answer assembly, citations, or multi-part responses. It overlooks the need for precise extraction and verification across document segments. 5. **E. Setting a very high max_tokens value, using a low temperature for summarization tasks, and caching all previous responses.** - Not optimal: Very high max_tokens increases cost and latency and can produce verbose, less focused answers. Low temperature for summarization is reasonable, but caching all responses is a practical maintenance concern and doesn’t address retrieval quality or scalability for diverse queries. Correct Proposition **B. Implementing intelligent document chunking strategies, employing few-shot learning in prompts, and configuring task-appropriate temperature settings.** Detailed Explanation 1. **Intelligent document chunking:** - Large documents exceed model context windows. Smart chunking preserves logical boundaries (sections, figures, tables) and maintains contextual integrity across chunks. - Proper chunking improves relevance of retrieved passages and reduces context fragmentation, leading to more accurate answers. 2. **Few-shot learning in prompts:** - Providing a few representative examples (Q&A pairs, style, and answer format) guides the model on how to interpret questions and format responses for the domain. - This reduces ambiguity and improves consistency across varying document types (policy docs, technical manuals, etc.). 3. **Task-appropriate temperature settings:** - Different tasks require different output characteristics: - Factual extraction and exactness: low temperature (deterministic). - Summarization or synthesis across multiple sections: slightly higher temperature to capture diverse angles while staying coherent. - Matching temperature to task improves precision, reliability, and usefulness of results. 4. **Why this combination over others:** - A overemphasizes model size and fixed prompts at the expense of cost, latency, and adaptability. - C pushes impractical processing and destabilizes output with high creativity in a QA context. - D and E lack comprehensive strategies for retrieval quality, answer assembly, and performance considerations, focusing too narrowly on embedding or output length without addressing aligned task behavior. Conclusion In designing a Cortex-based context-aware Q&A system for large documents, the combination in option B delivers reliable, scalable, and high-quality answers by aligning document handling (chunking), guidance (few-shot prompts), and output behavior (temperature) with the specific QA tasks. The most suitable choice is B.

An enterprise has a data pipeline built in Snowpark Python, a set of analytical dashboards using direct SQL queries, and a customer-facing web application. All three need to access the same Cortex LLM capabilities for different purposes. Which statement accurately describes the integration capabilities?
A. Cortex functions can only be called from within Snowflake SQL queries and are not accessible from external applications or Snowpark Python.
B. Cortex functions are primarily accessible via a dedicated REST API, which the SQL and Snowpark interfaces call internally.
Correct answer
C. Cortex LLM functions are accessible via SQL within Snowflake, Python through the Snowpark ML library, and REST APIs for external applications, providing multiple integration paths.
D. The only supported method for production applications is to use the Snowpark Container Services to host a model endpoint which then calls the Cortex functions.
E. Programmatic access is only available through JavaScript stored procedures, which can then be called from SQL or external applications.
Overall explanation
Option A - Claim: Cortex functions can only be called from within Snowflake SQL queries and are not accessible from external applications or Snowpark Python. - Analysis: This limitation would severely restrict practical use, as external apps and Snowpark Python are common integration paths. If Cortex were truly limited to Snowflake SQL, developers could not reuse it in data pipelines or in a web app without custom adapters. - Verdict: Likely incorrect. The typical design aims to support multiple access paths, not just SQL-only usage. Option B - Claim: Cortex functions are primarily accessible via a dedicated REST API, which the SQL and Snowpark interfaces call internally. - Analysis: If there is a dedicated REST API, that can enable external apps and Snowpark to access Cortex via HTTP calls. However, saying the REST API is the sole primary access path and that SQL/Snowpark “call internally” suggests a reduced direct surface for those interfaces. It’s plausible there are REST endpoints, but this wording may understate native SQL/Snowpark integrations. - Verdict: Potentially partially correct, but misleading if it implies SQL/Snowpark have no direct, native integration. Option C - Claim: Cortex LLM functions are accessible via SQL within Snowflake, Python through the Snowpark ML library, and REST APIs for external applications, providing multiple integration paths. - Analysis: - SQL within Snowflake: Direct availability of Cortex functions inside Snowflake queries enables seamless analytics workflows. - Snowpark Python: Access via Snowpark ML library allows Python-based data pipelines and transformations to call Cortex LLM capabilities. - REST APIs: External applications can invoke Cortex via standard HTTP REST calls, enabling web apps, services, and other runtimes to consume capabilities. - This aligns with a multi-path integration model, covering in-database (SQL), data engineering (Snowpark Python), and external application access (REST). - Verdict: Correct. It describes multiple, coherent integration paths that match typical Cortex deployment patterns. Option D - Claim: The only supported method for production applications is to use the Snowpark Container Services to host a model endpoint which then calls the Cortex functions. - Analysis: This is overly restrictive. If Cortex exposes SQL, Snowpark Python, and REST APIs, forcing a single Snowpark Container Services approach would limit flexibility and contradict an ecosystem designed for multiple access patterns. - Verdict: Incorrect. It imposes a single path rather than multiple integrations. Option E - Claim: Programmatic access is only available through JavaScript stored procedures, which can then be called from SQL or external applications. - Analysis: Limiting programmatic access to JavaScript stored procedures would exclude direct REST usage and Python/Snowpark access, which is unlikely for a modern LLM integration intended for breadth. - Verdict: Incorrect. It wrongly restricts access to a single scripting surface. Correct Proposition - The most accurate statement is: C. Cortex LLM functions are accessible via SQL within Snowflake, Python through the Snowpark ML library, and REST APIs for external applications, providing multiple integration paths. Detailed Explanation 1) Integration surfaces across use cases: - SQL in Snowflake: Enables analysts and BI workflows to invoke Cortex functions directly in queries, enabling model-assisted analytics without leaving SQL. - Snowpark Python: Allows data engineers and data scientists to build pipelines and ML workflows in Python while leveraging Cortex LLM capabilities through the Snowpark ML API, keeping the workflow in a familiar programming environment. - REST APIs: Exposes Cortex capabilities to any external application, microservice, or web app via standard HTTP, providing language-agnostic access and easy scoping for production services. 2) Why multiple paths matter for an enterprise: - A data pipeline, dashboards, and a customer-facing web app each have distinct integration requirements and runtime environments. A single access method would constrain development and deployment strategies. - REST APIs enable scalable, decoupled service architectures and easier governance, monitoring, and security controls for external apps. - Native SQL support tightens integration with Snowflake-based analytics, reducing data movement and enabling near real-time insights. - Snowpark Python integration accelerates developer productivity within the data engineering and data science teams, leveraging existing Python code and libraries. 3) Consistency with deployment design: - Providing multiple, coherent paths ensures that all three components (Snowpark-driven pipeline, direct SQL analytics, and external web apps) can uniformly leverage Cortex capabilities with consistent authentication, permissions, and billing models. 4) Practical implications: - Teams can choose the appropriate access path per use case without resorting to ad hoc adapters. - Security and governance can be centralized via the REST API layer while preserving the performance and convenience of in-database and Python-based access.

A developer is building a public-facing chatbot and needs to prevent the model from generating harmful or biased content. They intend to use the built-in Cortex Guard safety features. How should they activate these guardrails?
Correct answer
A. Set the account-level parameter CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED to TRUE to apply guardrails globally to all Cortex function calls.
B. Install a separate Snowflake native application called "Cortex Guard" and route all LLM requests through this application.
C. Specify the guardrails configuration within the options parameter of the COMPLETE function call, enabling features like PII detection and jailbreak prevention.
D. Create a masking policy that automatically redacts harmful content from the COMPLETE function's output.
E. Wrap every COMPLETE call in a try-catch block and use a secondary model to classify the output as safe or unsafe.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Set the account-level parameter CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED to TRUE to apply guardrails globally to all Cortex function calls. - Correct approach: This enables Cortex Guard protections at the account level, ensuring all function calls within the Cortex environment are subject to guardrails (e.g., PII detection, jailbreak prevention, content safety checks). It applies consistently across the system rather than on a per-call basis. 2. B. Install a separate Snowflake native application called "Cortex Guard" and route all LLM requests through this application. - Not applicable: Cortex Guard is a Cortex feature, not a separate Snowflake app. Routing through a separate app would complicate architecture and may not enable Cortex Guard protections as intended. 3. C. Specify the guardrails configuration within the options parameter of the COMPLETE function call, enabling features like PII detection and jailbreak prevention. - Partial correctness: While per-call options can influence behavior, guardrails are designed to be globally configured for consistency. Relying only on per-call options risks missing protections if a call bypasses the options or if defaults change. 4. D. Create a masking policy that automatically redacts harmful content from the COMPLETE function's output. - Narrow scope: Content masking is a mitigation but not a activation mechanism for Cortex Guard. It should be part of a broader guardrail strategy, not the primary activation method. 5. E. Wrap every COMPLETE call in a try-catch block and use a secondary model to classify the output as safe or unsafe. - Defensive pattern: Post-hoc classification can help catch issues, but it does not activate or enable Cortex Guard protections. It adds a safeguard but is not the recommended method to enable guardrails globally. ### Correct Proposition **The most appropriate method to activate Cortex Guard safety features is:** **A. Set the account-level parameter CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED to TRUE to apply guardrails globally to all Cortex function calls.** ### Detailed Explanation 1. Activation scope: - Setting CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED to TRUE enables guardrails at the account level, ensuring every Cortex function call (not just select calls) is subject to safety checks. This provides consistent protection across the entire environment. 2. What Cortex Guard does: - Cortex Guard includes protections like PII detection, jailbreak prevention, inappropriate content filters, and other policy checks. Global activation ensures these checks occur without relying on developer discipline per call. 3. Why not per-call options (option C): - While per-call options can tailor behavior, they are easy to forget or override, leading to inconsistent protection. Global activation reduces risk by ensuring guardrails are always in place. 4. Why other options are insufficient: - B introduces an incorrect integration approach. - D and E are mitigations, not activations; they do not enable the guardrails themselves and may add latency or complexity without guaranteeing comprehensive protection. - C could be used as a supplemental measure, but it should not be the sole activation mechanism. 5. Practical impact: - With A enabled, you get centralized, predictable safety enforcement, easier auditing, and users receive consistent safeguarding across all Cortex-enabled interactions.

Question 12
Skipped
Analyzing a complex query, a developer finds the following pattern used extensively: sql SELECT customer_id, SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.EMBED_TEXT_768( SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.SUMMARIZE(customer_feedback) ) AS feedback_embedding FROM customer_feedback_table; What is the primary performance and cost inefficiency in this pattern?
A. The SUMMARIZE function is less accurate than COMPLETE for generating text for embeddings.
B. It uses the 768-dimensional embedding model instead of the higher-dimensional 1024 model.
Correct answer
C. It nests an expensive LLM call (SUMMARIZE) inside another function (EMBED_TEXT_768), causing two separate Cortex charges per row where the summarized text output is immediately discarded.
D. The EMBED_TEXT_768 function should be called before the SUMMARIZE function to improve context.
E. It does not use a WHERE clause to filter the data, so it processes all rows unnecessarily.
Overall explanation
Correct proposition: C Detailed explanations for each option A. The SUMMARIZE function is less accurate than COMPLETE for generating text for embeddings. - Why this is not addressing performance/cost: This statement compares accuracy, not how the query is executed or billed. The question focuses on performance and cost inefficiency, not model quality. - Implication: Even if SUMMARIZE were less accurate, that does not explain higher compute/time charges per row. The billed activity is determined by the number and type of Cortex calls, not necessarily the relative accuracy of the content produced. B. It uses the 768-dimensional embedding model instead of the higher-dimensional 1024 model. - Why not the main issue: This is about embedding dimensionality, which affects vector size and downstream storage/compute for similarity/search. It does not explain why there would be two Cortex charges per row or why the pattern is inefficient in this specific construct. - Implication: While a 1024-dim model might be more expensive per embedding, the core inefficiency here is the nesting of a heavy LLM step inside another Cortex function, not the dimensionality choice of the embedding. C. It nests an expensive LLM call (SUMMARIZE) inside another function (EMBED_TEXT_768), causing two separate Cortex charges per row where the summarized text output is immediately discarded. - Correct reason: This is exactly the performance/cost issue. - SUMMARIZE is an LLM-driven operation that incurs a Cortex charge. - EMBED_TEXT_768 is another Cortex operation that computes embeddings (also billed). - The query uses EMBED_TEXT_768 on the output of SUMMARIZE, but if the summarized text is not needed downstream (and is effectively discarded after embedding), you’re paying for both: the summarization step and the embedding step, per row. - This doubles the Cortex billing per row for work that could often be avoided by embedding the original text or by composing the operations more efficiently (e.g., embed the source text directly or reuse a cached/filtered representation). - Additionally, performing two expensive operations in sequence per row increases latency and compute cost without a proportional gain in value. D. The EMBED_TEXT_768 function should be called before the SUMMARIZE function to improve context. - Why not: The proposed reordering would still incur two Cortex charges per row (one for the embedding, one for the summarization) and may even reduce the utility if embedding the raw text first isn’t aligned with the intended downstream use. The fundamental inefficiency is the per-row dual Cortex call, not the order. If the goal is to reduce cost, you’d avoid one of the expensive calls or cache results, rather than simply swapping order. E. It does not use a WHERE clause to filter the data, so it processes all rows unnecessarily. - Why not: While filtering can improve performance by reducing processed rows, the primary, most acute inefficiency highlighted by the pattern is the nested expensive operations (two Cortex calls per row). Even with filtering, if those two calls remain per row for the filtered subset, costs persist. The main issue is the dual, nested Cortex calls whose results may be discarded, not the lack of a filter. Conclusion - The primary performance and cost inefficiency is described in C: nesting an expensive LLM call (SUMMARIZE) inside another function (EMBED_TEXT_768), leading to two Cortex charges per row with potentially unused summarized text. This pattern directly inflates compute cost and latency.

Question 13
Skipped
An administrator wants to create a monthly report showing the average cost incurred per call of the COMPLETE function. Which query against the ACCOUNT_USAGE views will correctly calculate this metric?
A. SELECT SUM(credits_used) / COUNT(query_id) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';
Correct answer
B. SELECT AVG(credits_used) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';
C. SELECT total_tokens / total_credits FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY;
D. SELECT SUM(credits_used) FROM WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY WHERE service_type = 'CORTEX_LLM';
E. SELECT COUNT(query_id) / SUM(credits_used) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';
Overall explanation
Correct Proposition B. SELECT AVG(credits_used) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE'; Reasoning - The metric "average cost incurred per call" means: average credits consumed per invocation of the COMPLETE function. - Each row in CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY represents usage of a function call with credits_used indicating the cost for that call. - AVG(credits_used) computes the mean of credits_used across all rows that match function_name = 'COMPLETE', which is exactly the average cost per call. - Other options: - A: SUM(credits_used) / COUNT(query_id) also yields an average per call, but relies on query_id counting and can be less robust if there are edge cases (e.g., missing rows). AVG(credits_used) is simpler and clearer for per-call average. - C: total_tokens / total_credits is unrelated to per-call average and mixes tokens with credits, not matching the requested metric. - D: SUM(credits_used) FROM WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY WHERE service_type = 'CORTEX_LLM' looks at a different metering history and is not scoped to function invocations. - E: COUNT(query_id) / SUM(credits_used) is the reciprocal of the desired ratio (calls per credit), not credits per call. Notes - To produce a true monthly report, apply a time filter (e.g., where usage_timestamp between month_start and month_end) or aggregate by month as needed, e.g.: - SELECT AVG(credits_used) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE' AND usage_timestamp >= 'YYYY-MM-01' AND usage_timestamp < 'YYYY-MM-01' + INTERVAL '1 month';

Question 14
Skipped
A data pipeline is designed to process 50,000 documents using three Cortex functions: CLASSIFY_TEXT, SUMMARIZE, and EXTRACT_ANSWER. The current query runs all three functions on every document. What optimization would most significantly reduce processing time and cost without sacrificing necessary business insights?
A. Increase the size of the warehouse executing the query to parallelize the workload further.
B. Remove the EXTRACT_ANSWER function call as it is redundant if SUMMARIZE is used.
Correct answer
C. Run the CLASSIFY_TEXT function first on all documents, then based on the document type, selectively run SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER only on the relevant subsets.
D. Modify the SUMMARIZE function's max_tokens parameter to a lower value to reduce output token consumption.
E. Pre-filter the documents to only process those created in the last 30 days.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Increase the size of the warehouse executing the query to parallelize the workload further.** - This would likely improve raw throughput only if the bottleneck is in the warehouse’s compute capacity. However, it increases cost and may not reduce the number of function invocations or the overall work that must be done. It also doesn’t eliminate unnecessary processing or reduce the total amount of data each document requires, so the business insights may remain unchanged while costs rise. 2. **B. Remove the EXTRACT_ANSWER function call as it is redundant if SUMMARIZE is used.** - This is incorrect because SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER serve different purposes. SUMMARIZE creates a concise summary of the document’s content; EXTRACT_ANSWER pulls specific answers or data points. Removing EXTRACT_ANSWER would lose targeted insights, potentially degrading business value. 3. **C. Run the CLASSIFY_TEXT function first on all documents, then based on the document type, selectively run SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER only on the relevant subsets.** - This optimizes by front-loading a lightweight classification to funnel only relevant documents into heavier processing (SUMMARIZE/EXTRACT_ANSWER). It reduces total function invocations and token consumption for documents where those steps aren’t needed, cutting both time and cost while preserving or even improving business insights by targeting processing to content that requires it. 4. **D. Modify the SUMMARIZE function's max_tokens parameter to a lower value to reduce output token consumption.** - While lowering max_tokens saves tokens and cost for summaries, it may degrade the usefulness of the summaries and could require reprocessing or additional steps to extract needed insights. It doesn’t reduce the number of documents that reach SUMMARIZE or EXTRACT_ANSWER, so overall cost and time may not be minimized as effectively as option C. 5. **E. Pre-filter the documents to only process those created in the last 30 days.** - This reduces the volume of documents processed but risks missing long-tail insights or trends older than 30 days that might still be relevant for business needs. It’s a blunt cutoff that could sacrifice value for time savings. Correct Proposition **C. Run the CLASSIFY_TEXT function first on all documents, then based on the document type, selectively run SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER only on the relevant subsets.** Detailed Explanation 1. Bottleneck identification: - The heaviest costs come from running SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER on every document, regardless of whether those steps are needed for a given document. 2. How option C helps: - CLASSIFY_TEXT acts as a lightweight routing step. It quickly categorizes documents so you can determine which documents actually require deeper processing. - By conditionally invoking SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER only on subsets, you avoid unnecessary work on documents that don’t need those steps, drastically reducing both compute time and token usage. 3. Impact on business insights: - Maintains or improves value by ensuring deeper processing is applied where it matters most, based on content type or category. - Prevents wasteful processing of irrelevant documents while keeping the ability to extract precise answers and produce summaries for eligible items. 4. Cost and time considerations: - Reduces the number of expensive function calls and the total token volume processed. - Scales better with larger document volumes since the classification step is inexpensive and guides selective processing. 5. Why not the other options: - A increases cost without addressing unnecessary work. - B eliminates a potentially valuable capability (EXTRACT_ANSWER). - D compromises result quality for only token savings. - E risks losing important long-term insights.

Question 15
Skipped
Which THREE of the following statements accurately describe token consumption and billing for Cortex functions?
Correct selection
A. The VECTOR_COSINE_SIMILARITY function consumes Cortex credits based on the dimension of the vectors being compared.
Correct selection
B. The EMBED_TEXT_768 function charges are based on the number of input tokens in the text being embedded.
Correct selection
C. The COMPLETE function charges for the sum of both input (prompt) and output (completion) tokens.
D. The TRANSLATE function has a fixed cost per call, independent of the text length.
E. The CLASSIFY_TEXT function's cost is determined by the number of categories provided in the input list.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. The VECTOR_COSINE_SIMILARITY function consumes Cortex credits based on the dimension of the vectors being compared.** - Likely true. Cosine similarity between high-dimensional vectors typically incurs more compute as the vector dimension increases, which can translate into higher credit consumption in vector-based Cortex functions. 2. **B. The EMBED_TEXT_768 function charges are based on the number of input tokens in the text being embedded.** - Likely true. Embedding functions are commonly priced per input token. The “768” likely refers to the embedding size/type, but charges are typically tied to how many input tokens are processed, aligning with per-token billing. 3. **C. The COMPLETE function charges for the sum of both input (prompt) and output (completion) tokens.** - Likely true. In many text-generation APIs, pricing covers the total tokens processed: tokens fed to the model (prompt) plus tokens generated in the response (completion). 4. **D. The TRANSLATE function has a fixed cost per call, independent of the text length.** - Likely false. Translation typically scales with text length (tokens), not a fixed per-call charge, since longer texts require more processing. 5. **E. The CLASSIFY_TEXT function's cost is determined by the number of categories provided in the input list.** - Likely false. While some classification schemas may consider the number of categories, most token-based billing models price by input tokens and/or output labels, not simply by the count of categories. The cost being strictly a function of the number of categories is unlikely. Correct Propositions **A, B, and C** are the statements that most plausibly describe token consumption and billing for Cortex functions.
