Question 16
Skipped
An administrator needs to generate a report detailing which specific users are making the most expensive calls to the COMPLETE function, including the model used and the number of tokens consumed per call. Which ACCOUNT_USAGE view provides the most granular data required for this analysis?
A. QUERY_HISTORY
Correct answer
B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY
C. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY
D. DATABASE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY
E. AUTOMATIC_CLUSTERING_HISTORY
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. QUERY_HISTORY** - Why it’s not the best fit: QUERY_HISTORY logs per-query execution details (text, start time, duration, users, warehouse, etc.). It does not natively expose per-call token counts or integration with Cortex/LLM models. It’s useful for general query auditing but lacks granularity for model/token usage of a specific function like COMPLETE. - When it could help: If you mainly needed who ran queries and basic performance, not the model/token details. 2. **B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY** - Why this is the best fit: This view is designed to capture usage data for Cortex-generated functions, including details about the function calls, the models used, and likely token/usage metrics per call. It aligns directly with tracking expensive COMPLETE function invocations, and it provides the granularity needed (per-call, per-user, per-model, per-token). - When it’s essential: To report which users invoke the COMPLETE function most, identify the model used for each invocation, and quantify tokens consumed per call. 3. **C. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY** - Why it’s not the best fit: This view reports credits consumed by warehouses (compute costs) over time. It’s excellent for cost planning and per-warehouse consumption but does not break down usage by function calls, models, or per-call token details. - When it could help: For high-level cost attribution to warehouses, not for per-function usage analytics. 4. **D. DATABASE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY** - Why it’s not the best fit: This view shows storage consumption by databases/schemas/tables over time. It has no relevance to function invocation metrics, models, or tokens. - When it could help: For storage cost reporting, not for function invocation analytics. 5. **E. AUTOMATIC_CLUSTERING_HISTORY** - Why it’s not the best fit: This view tracks automatic clustering activity and maintenance events on tables. It does not provide data about function executions, models used, or token counts. - When it could help: For monitoring or auditing clustering behavior, not for function invocation analytics. Conclusion Correct Proposition: **B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY** Detailed rationale: To generate a report detailing which specific users are making the most expensive calls to the COMPLETE function, including the model used and tokens consumed per call, you need the per-invocation telemetry provided by Cortex usage history. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY is specifically designed to capture per-call details for Cortex-enabled functions, including user identifiers, the model invoked, and tokenized usage metrics, enabling precise, granular analysis of expensive COMPLETE function calls. The other views lack either per-call function usage granularity (A), or any information about Cortex function calls, models, and tokens (C, D, E).
Domain
Knowledge Area: ACCOUNT_USAGE
Question 17
Skipped
A company has 250,000 customer support tickets and wants to identify the most frequently reported software bugs and feature requests. Which Cortex-based approach will yield the most specific and actionable insights for the product development teams?
A. Use SENTIMENT on all tickets and flag those with a strongly negative score for manual review.
B. Use EMBED_TEXT to create embeddings for all tickets, cluster them, and then manually label the clusters.
C. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize each ticket as either "Bug" or "Feature Request".
Correct answer
D. Use COMPLETE with a prompt designed to extract structured data (e.g., bug name, component, severity) from each ticket, then aggregate the results to find trends.
E. Use SUMMARIZE on all tickets and then run a word frequency analysis on the summaries.
Overall explanation
Option analysis 1. A. Use SENTIMENT on all tickets and flag those with a strongly negative score for manual review. - Strengths: Highlights broadly negative sentiment, which can surface unhappy customers. - Limitations: Negative sentiment alone doesn’t tell you whether the issue is a bug or a feature request, nor which product area, severity, or component is affected. It leads to manual triage of many tickets that may not be actionable or actionable in a structured way. It also risks overlooking specific, high-signal issues that aren’t strongly negative in sentiment. 2. B. Use EMBED_TEXT to create embeddings for all tickets, cluster them, and then manually label the clusters. - Strengths: Unsupervised grouping can reveal natural themes and topics at scale, including bug-like and feature-like themes. - Limitations: Clustering results require substantial manual interpretation, labeling effort, and upkeep. While it surfaces themes, it may be hard to translate clusters into concrete, structured data (e.g., bug name, component, severity) without extensive downstream work. Actionability depends on the labeling quality and consistency. 3. C. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize each ticket as either "Bug" or "Feature Request". - Strengths: Simple, scalable categorization that can separate bugs from feature requests, enabling targeted workflows. - Limitations: This binary split lacks granularity (e.g., which component, severity, reproduce steps) and may miss nuanced insights within each category. It’s a good first step, but not sufficient for deeply actionable product insights without further enrichment. 4. D. Use COMPLETE with a prompt designed to extract structured data (e.g., bug name, component, severity) from each ticket, then aggregate the results to find trends. - Strengths: Produces structured, machine-readable data directly (bug name, component, severity, reproduction steps, version, impact, etc.). Enables precise aggregation, trend detection, and prioritization for product teams. Scales well, reduces manual labeling, and provides a clear blueprint for downstream analytics and roadmapping. - Limitations: Requires well-crafted prompts and validation to ensure data quality. May need domain-specific prompts per product area, and occasional human review for edge cases. 5. E. Use SUMMARIZE on all tickets and then run a word frequency analysis on the summaries. - Strengths: Produces concise overviews of tickets, which can reveal common terms and themes. - Limitations: Summaries can omit critical structured details (component, severity, steps to reproduce). Word frequency can be noisy (common words, synonyms, lingo) and may require normalization. Overall, it’s more exploratory and less directly actionable for structured prioritization. Correct proposition The most suitable and actionable approach is: D. Use COMPLETE with a prompt designed to extract structured data (e.g., bug name, component, severity) from each ticket, then aggregate the results to find trends. Detailed rationale - Why D over A: Sentiment alone identifies unhappy tickets but doesn’t yield structured, actionable fields (component, severity, bug vs feature, reproducibility). Sentiment is a coarse signal and often requires manual triage before it can drive product decisions. - Why D over B: Clustering reveals themes but doesn’t guarantee consistent, structured extraction of key attributes. It requires additional labeling and design to convert clusters into structured data. D directly produces structured fields that are immediately aggregatable. - Why D over C: Classifying as Bug/Feature is helpful but insufficient for prioritization because it lacks detailed attributes (which component, how severe, where it occurs). D provides a richer schema for downstream analysis. - Why D over E: Summarization plus word frequency can surface themes but falls short on reliability and actionability due to potential loss of important details and the noise in raw language. D yields precise, use-case-ready data for trends and roadmaps. - Practical impact: By extracting structured data (bug name, component, severity, environment, version, steps to reproduce, etc.) from each ticket, product teams can quantitatively aggregate issues, identify top bug areas, most-requested features by component, and set data-driven priorities. This approach scales to 250,000 tickets and provides a clean pipeline into bug triage, backlog grooming, and release planning.
Domain
Knowledge area: Text analytics and structured data extractio
Question 18
Skipped
An application is deployed on Snowpark Container Services (SPCS). This application internally calls the SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE function. To perform a complete audit of the total cost of running this application, which THREE ACCOUNT_USAGE views must be queried?
Correct selection
A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY (for the SPCS compute pool costs)
Correct selection
B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY (for the serverless LLM inference costs)
C. STAGE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY (for the container image storage costs)
Correct selection
D. QUERY_HISTORY (for the SQL query execution costs)
E. CORTEX_SEARCH_DAILY_USAGE_HISTORY (for the semantic search indexing costs)
Overall explanation
Here’s the detailed evaluation of each proposition and the correct selections. Correct propositions - A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY - B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY - D. QUERY_HISTORY Why these are correct - A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY (for the SPCS compute pool costs) - Purpose: Tracks compute usage and credits consumed by warehouses over time. - Relevance: Snowpark Container Services (SPCS) runs on a compute warehouse; to audit total cost, you must include the warehouse credits consumed by the SPCS compute pool. - Why needed: This is the primary source of deterministic compute-cost data for any workload, including an app that uses Snowflake resources via SPCS. - B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY (for the serverless LLM inference costs) - Purpose: Records usage and costs associated with Snowflake Cortex functions, including serverless inference. - Relevance: The application calls SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE, which incurs Cortex usage costs. To fully audit total cost, you must include Cortex usage data. - Why needed: Without this, you’d miss the portion of spend tied to Cortex-based inference/LLM operations invoked by the application. - D. QUERY_HISTORY (for the SQL query execution costs) - Purpose: Logs executed queries, their runtimes, and related resources; each query contributes to warehouse credits. - Relevance: Even though warehouse metering captures the credits, QUERY_HISTORY provides visibility into the query workload that drove those costs and helps corroborate compute usage at the SQL level. - Why needed: Provides a complete picture of query-level activity and serves as a cross-check for overall cost attribution tied to specific queries and workloads. Why the other options are not correct (briefly) - C. STAGE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY (for the container image storage costs) - Relevance: Only covers storage costs for stages (e.g., container images). If the question is about total cost for running the app, storage costs may be a component, but the three required views focus on compute and Cortex usage primarily. However, depending on scope, stage storage could be considered; by default, the trio asked for typically emphasizes compute and Cortex usage (A, B, D). - E. CORTEX_SEARCH_DAILY_USAGE_HISTORY (for the semantic search indexing costs) - Relevance: Specific to Cortex search/indexing usage, not necessarily the primary cost driver for a general app using CORTEX.COMPLETE unless semantic search is a defined part of the workload. Not the standard trio for “total cost of running this application” unless semantic search is explicitly involved. Final answer - The three ACCOUNT_USAGE views to query are: A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY, B. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY, and D. QUERY_HISTORY.

Question 19
Skipped
To maintain data governance, an organization needs to ensure that only authorized data engineers can create new Cortex Search services, preventing the indexing of sensitive tables. What is the most precise privilege control to implement this policy?
A. Revoke the CREATE SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from all roles except authorized ones.
B. Apply row-level security policies on the underlying source tables to prevent unauthorized access.
Correct answer
C. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.
D. Use the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST parameter to restrict which models can be used for indexing.
E. Implement a masking policy on all columns that should not be indexed.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Revoke the CREATE SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from all roles except authorized ones.** - Pros: Targets the ability to create search services at the schema level and restricts to authorized roles. - Cons: Uses a generic term “SEARCH SERVICE” instead of the specific Cortex Search capability. If the privilege name is actually “CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE,” this option may be misaligned with the exact privilege nomenclature and could be ineffective in practice. 2. **B. Apply row-level security policies on the underlying source tables to prevent unauthorized access.** - Pros: Improves data access control at the data row level. - Cons: Does not directly control who can create Cortex Search services. It governs data visibility, not the act of indexing or service creation. Therefore it does not address the policy of preventing indexing of sensitive tables through Cortex Search. 3. **C. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.** - Pros: Precisely targets the capability to create Cortex Search services. By revoking this privilege from unauthorized roles, you prevent any user from provisioning new Cortex Search services on that schema, directly aligning with the policy goal. Cons: Requires correct privilege naming and scope (schema-level). If the privilege is granted at a different scope (e.g., account-wide or database-wide), you’d need to revoke at the appropriate scope. 4. **D. Use the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST parameter to restrict which models can be used for indexing.** - Pros: Controls which models can be indexed, enhancing model-level governance. - Cons: Does not prevent creation of Cortex Search services themselves or indexing into sensitive tables by unauthorized users. It’s about which models can be used, not who can create services. 5. **E. Implement a masking policy on all columns that should not be indexed.** - Pros: Hides sensitive data during queries, reducing exposure. - Cons: Masking columns does not stop the creation of Cortex Search services or indexing. It mitigates data exposure but does not address governance over who can create services or index, making it insufficient for the stated policy. Correct Proposition **C. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.** Detailed Explanation 1. Policy goal alignment: - You want to ensure that only authorized data engineers can create new Cortex Search services, thereby preventing indexing of sensitive tables. The direct and precise way to enforce this is to control the privilege that permits creating Cortex Search services. 2. Privilege specificity: - The relevant privilege is “CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE” (schema-scoped). Revoking this privilege from unauthorized roles prevents those roles from provisioning new Cortex Search services on that schema. This is exactly the mechanism needed to stop unauthorized indexing setup. 3. Scope considerations: - Apply the revocation at the appropriate scope (schema). If the privilege could be granted at broader scopes (database, account), ensure revocation occurs at the lowest authoritative scope that governs Cortex Search service creation for the targeted schema. 4. Why not other options: - A uses a generic or possibly incorrect privilege name and may not map to the actual Cortex-specific grant. - B addresses data access, not service creation. - D restricts model usage, not ability to create services. - E masks data, but does not prevent service creation or indexing actions. Conclusion To enforce precise control over who can create Cortex Search services and thereby prevent indexing of sensitive tables, revoke the specific Cortex Search creation privilege from unauthorized roles: C. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.

Question 20
Skipped
A team is using a Cortex function inside a query that scans a large table. They notice the query is slow and consumes a high number of credits. The query pattern involves calling the function on a column without any filtering. What is the MOST impactful optimization?
A. Change the Cortex function to a smaller, faster model.
B. Use a result cache to avoid recomputing the function on unchanged data.
Correct answer
C. Apply a WHERE clause to reduce the number of rows processed by the Cortex function.
D. Increase the warehouse size to process the function calls in parallel.
E. Rewrite the query to use a JavaScript UDF instead of the Cortex function.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Change the Cortex function to a smaller, faster model** - Pros: A smaller model can reduce per-call compute time, which may help if the bottleneck is the Cortex inference latency. - Cons: The query still scans a large table and calls the function on a large set of rows. If you don’t filter the data, you’re paying per-row or per-call cost regardless of model speed. The overall credits and latency remain dominated by the number of rows processed, not just the model’s speed. 2. **B. Use a result cache to avoid recomputing the function on unchanged data** - Pros: If the same inputs are reprocessed, caching can avoid duplicate work and cut credits for repeated runs. - Cons: In a pattern where the query scans a large table with little or no filtering, many inputs will differ between runs (or caches may quickly become invalid). Caching adds complexity and may not effectively reduce the cost for initial large scans. 3. **C. Apply a WHERE clause to reduce the number of rows processed by the Cortex function** - Pros: Directly reduces the number of rows the Cortex function must evaluate, which linearly lowers compute and credits. This addresses the root cause when no filtering is applied. - Cons: Requires identifying appropriate predicates (filters) that preserve result correctness. May require indexing or data organization to be effective. 4. **D. Increase the warehouse size to process the function calls in parallel** - Pros: More parallelism can lower wall-clock time for large workloads. - Cons: Higher credits typically result from larger warehouses, and the total compute cost may increase even if wall-clock time decreases. If the bottleneck is per-row processing, simply adding parallelism does not reduce the total work done. 5. **E. Rewrite the query to use a JavaScript UDF instead of the Cortex function** - Pros: Might offer different optimization opportunities or integration. - Cons: Likely slower or more expensive per-row, and generally adds complexity. Does not inherently reduce the amount of data processed. Correct Proposition **C. Apply a WHERE clause to reduce the number of rows processed by the Cortex function** Detailed Explanation 1. Bottleneck identification: - The pattern involves calling a Cortex function on a column without filtering, meaning every row in a large table triggers a Cortex inference. This drives the majority of compute credits and latency. 2. Impact of filtering: - Introducing a WHERE clause reduces the input set to the Cortex function. Fewer rows processed means fewer per-row inferences, which directly reduces compute credits and total query time. - This change scales well: as you add or tighten filters, the Cortex workload shrinks proportionally. 3. Why not other options as the primary fix: - A (smaller model): Helpful for per-call cost, but does not address the volume of calls. If the same data volume is still processed, total credits remain high. - B (result cache): Beneficial only if the same exact inputs are processed repeatedly. In many large scans with varying inputs, cache hit rate is low. - D (larger warehouse): Increases parallelism and can reduce wall-clock time, but raises compute credits and may not reduce total work; not addressing the fundamental issue of processing many rows. - E (JS UDF): Often slower and more expensive per-row; adds complexity without necessarily reducing total work. 4. Practical considerations for implementing C: - Identify relevant predicates that preserve result semantics. - Ensure proper statistics or indexes exist to support selective filtering. - Validate that the filtered result matches business requirements. - Monitor after changes to confirm reductions in runtime and credits. 5. Expected outcome: - With a well-chosen WHERE clause, the Cortex function processes far fewer rows, leading to substantial reductions in latency and total compute credits, delivering the most impactful optimization for this scenario.
Domain
Knowledge area: Query performance optimization (cost-aware).
Question 21
Skipped
Regarding the fine-tuning of foundation models in Snowflake Cortex, which of the following statements is true?
A. Fine-tuning modifies the original base model in-place, and the original model is no longer accessible.
B. Fine-tuning requires the training data to be stored in a stage as Parquet files, not in a Snowflake table.
C. Fine-tuning is a synchronous operation that blocks the SQL session until the job is complete.
Correct answer
D. Fine-tuning creates a new, custom model variant that can be referenced by the name provided in the job options.
E. Fine-tuning is only available for the largest models (e.g., llama3.1-405b) and not for smaller models.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Fine-tuning modifies the original base model in-place, and the original model is no longer accessible.** - False. Fine-tuning in Cortex creates a new variant; the base model remains unchanged and accessible. You reference and use the new fine-tuned variant by its name, while the original base model stays intact. 2. **B. Fine-tuning requires the training data to be stored in a stage as Parquet files, not in a Snowflake table.** - Partially false/unsure in phrasing. Cortex typically uses data stored in a Snowflake stage and can leverage Parquet format for efficient ingestion, but the statement as written is too absolute about a prohibition on using Snowflake tables. The canonical requirement is that data must be accessible to the job (via stage or table) and typically ingested in a columnar format (like Parquet) for performance, but the strict “not in a Snowflake table” aspect makes this claim incorrect as stated. 3. **C. Fine-tuning is a synchronous operation that blocks the SQL session until the job is complete.** - False. Fine-tuning is executed as an asynchronous job. It does not block the SQL session in the sense that you submit a job and subsequently continue other work or poll for status; the session itself is not blocked until completion. 4. **D. Fine-tuning creates a new, custom model variant that can be referenced by the name provided in the job options.** - True. The service creates a new model variant that you can reference by the name you supply in the tuning job configuration. This is the standard behavior: you get a distinct, named fine-tuned model separate from the base. 5. **E. Fine-tuning is only available for the largest models (e.g., llama3.1-405b) and not for smaller models.** - False. Fine-tuning support typically extends to a range of models, not solely the largest. It is designed to enable customization across multiple model sizes, not exclusively the biggest ones. Correct Proposition **D. Fine-tuning creates a new, custom model variant that can be referenced by the name provided in the job options.** Detailed Explanation 1. **Understanding fine-tuning in Snowflake Cortex:** - Fine-tuning is the process of training a model further on domain-specific data to produce a specialized variant. This does not alter the original base model; instead, it yields a new, distinct model variant that inherits the base capabilities but with domain-specific adaptations. 2. **Model lifecycle and accessibility:** - The base model remains accessible for standard tasks. The fine-tuned variant is referenced by the name you specify when submitting the tuning job. This separation ensures you can reuse or revert to the base model if needed. 3. **Data input considerations:** - Training data must be accessible to the tuning job. In practice, Cortex supports data ingestion from Snowflake stages (often leveraging Parquet for efficient, columnar storage) or other accessible sources. The key point is availability and format suitable for ingestion, rather than a hard, exclusive constraint that data must be “Parquet-only” in a stage and not in a Snowflake table. 4. **Asynchronous processing model:** - Fine-tuning runs as an asynchronous operation. You submit the job and then monitor its status (e.g., via a job ID). The SQL session itself is not blocked in a way that prevents other operations; you can issue subsequent commands and poll for completion. 5. **Model size applicability:** - Fine-tuning support generally spans multiple model sizes. It is not restricted only to the largest models, allowing customization across a spectrum of model variants. Conclusion The true statement is D: Fine-tuning creates a new, custom model variant that can be referenced by the name provided in the job options. The other options contain inaccuracies about in-place modification (A), data format/availability constraints (B), synchronous blocking (C), or model size scope (E).
Domain
Knowledge area: Foundation models — fine-tuning in Snowflake
Question 22
Skipped
A developer wants to use the SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE function to generate SQL code based on natural language questions. They want the generated SQL to be syntactically correct for Snowflake. Which strategy is most likely to improve the quality of the generated SQL code consistently?
A. Use the mistral-7b model because it is the most cost-effective for code generation.
B. Set the temperature parameter to 1.0 to encourage creative and diverse SQL solutions.
Correct answer
C. Use a few-shot learning prompt that includes 2-3 examples of natural language questions and their corresponding correct Snowflake SQL queries.
D. Use the EXTRACT_ANSWER function instead, specifying the question "What is the SQL query?".
E. First use CLASSIFY_TEXT to determine the type of SQL operation (SELECT, INSERT, etc.) needed.
Overall explanation
Propositions analysis 1. A. Use the mistral-7b model because it is the most cost-effective for code generation - Why it’s weak: Cost-effectiveness does not guarantee higher quality or correctness of Snowflake SQL. Model choice alone, especially focused on cost, does not address alignment with Snowflake syntax, conventions, or the need for consistent, correct outputs. - Impact on quality: Limited. A cheaper model may produce less accurate or less consistent SQL, which hurts reliability in code generation tasks. 2. B. Set the temperature parameter to 1.0 to encourage creative and diverse SQL solutions - Why it’s weak: A high temperature increases randomness, which can reduce determinism and correctness. For code generation, you want reproducible, accurate results rather than highly varied attempts. - Impact on quality: Negative for consistency. Creativity can produce valid variants, but it also raises the risk of syntactic errors or non-optimal queries. 3. C. Use a few-shot learning prompt that includes 2-3 examples of natural language questions and their corresponding correct Snowflake SQL queries - Why it’s strong: Few-shot prompts provide concrete examples of the mapping from NL to correct Snowflake SQL, which directly guides the model toward producing syntactically valid and semantically correct queries. It helps establish style, syntax conventions, and expected structures (SELECT, FROM, WHERE, etc.), improving consistency across questions. - Impact on quality: High. Grounding the model with representative examples reduces ambiguity and improves accuracy and reliability in generated queries. 4. D. Use the EXTRACT_ANSWER function instead, specifying the question "What is the SQL query?" - Why it’s weak: EXTRACT_ANSWER is not a standard or universally applicable method for improving SQL generation quality. It suggests a post-processing or extraction step, which may not address underlying generation accuracy or correctness. It also relies on an external function that may not interact predictably with the Snowflake SQL generator. - Impact on quality: Uncertain and likely limited. It does not provide direct guidance to improve how the SQL is formed. 5. E. First use CLASSIFY_TEXT to determine the type of SQL operation (SELECT, INSERT, etc.) needed - Why it’s helpful but incomplete: Classifying the operation type can help route the generation path and ensure the right structure (e.g., SELECT vs. INSERT). However, on its own, it doesn’t teach the model how to produce correct and SQL-idiomatic queries or provide concrete examples of correct syntax. - Impact on quality: Moderate. It can be a useful preprocessing step, but without examples or constraints, it may not substantially improve consistency or correctness of the actual SQL output. Correct proposition C. Use a few-shot learning prompt that includes 2-3 examples of natural language questions and their corresponding correct Snowflake SQL queries Detailed rationale - Why few-shot prompts work well here: - They establish concrete mappings from NL intents to Snowflake SQL constructs, showing exact syntax, functions, and conventions that are expected. - They reduce ambiguity about how to structure queries (e.g., which clauses to include, ordering, quoting, and dialect-specific options). - They improve consistency across diverse NL questions by providing a template and salt-bridge examples the model can imitate. - What to include in a strong few-shot prompt: - A variety of representative NL questions covering common operations (SELECT with WHERE, JOINs, aggregation, subqueries) and edge cases. - Corresponding Snowflake SQL queries that are syntactically correct and idiomatic. - Clear mapping notes if needed (e.g., how to handle aliases, schema qualification, and parameter placeholders). - Expected impact on Snowflake SQL quality: - Increased syntactic correctness and alignment with Snowflake conventions. - Higher reliability and determinism in outputs, aiding easier testing and deployment. - Reduced need for post-generation corrections, saving time and reducing risk. Conclusion The most effective strategy for consistently improving the quality of generated Snowflake SQL code is option C: use a few-shot learning prompt with 2-3 examples of NL questions and their correct Snowflake SQL queries.
Domain
Knowledge area: Prompt engineering — few-shot learning for c
Question 23
Skipped
Which of the following scenarios would likely result in the highest cost for using the SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.EMBED_TEXT_768 function?
A. Embedding 10,000 product names, each averaging 5 words.
B. Embedding 1,000 full-text news articles, each averaging 5,000 words.
C. Embedding 100,000 unique customer email addresses.
D. Embedding 50,000 short social media posts, each averaging 20 words.
Correct answer
E. Embedding 500 long legal contracts, each averaging 50,000 words.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Embedding 10,000 product names, each averaging 5 words (total ~50,000 words)** - Total data: about 50,000 words. - Impact on cost: Relatively low compared to other scenarios because the aggregate text length is small. The function’s cost likely scales with total tokens/words processed, so this scenario would incur a modest cost. 2. **B. Embedding 1,000 full-text news articles, each averaging 5,000 words (total ~5,000,000 words)** - Total data: about 5 million words. - Impact on cost: Significantly higher than A due to a large increase in total word count. Still far below the worst-case option, but it represents a substantial usage footprint and higher compute and storage costs. 3. **C. Embedding 100,000 unique customer email addresses (total ~100,000 words)** - Total data: about 100,000 words. - Impact on cost: Higher than A but far lower than B, D, or E. This is a moderate workload with limited scale, resulting in a moderate cost impact. 4. **D. Embedding 50,000 short social media posts, each averaging 20 words (total ~1,000,000 words)** - Total data: about 1 million words. - Impact on cost: Higher than A and C, but lower than B and E. It represents a sizable but not extreme workload, leading to a noticeable but not maximal cost increase. 5. **E. Embedding 500 long legal contracts, each averaging 50,000 words (total ~25,000,000 words)** - Total data: about 25 million words. - Impact on cost: Likely the highest among all options. This scenario involves a massive total word count, resulting in the greatest compute, memory, and storage usage, thus the highest cost. ### Correct Proposition **E. Embedding 500 long legal contracts, each averaging 50,000 words.** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Cost drivers in text embeddings:** - Most embeddings services charge based on total input tokens/words processed and sometimes by output token usage. More text means more computations and larger intermediate representations, driving up cost. 2. **Total word count comparison:** - A ≈ 50,000 words - B ≈ 5,000,000 words - C ≈ 100,000 words - D ≈ 1,000,000 words - E ≈ 25,000,000 words - E has a 500x larger word count than A, and roughly 5x larger than B, making it the dominant cost factor. 3. **Resource implications:** - Higher word counts require more GPU/CPU processing time, memory, and potentially longer queue times. Storage of embeddings also scales with the number of tokens and resulting vector dimensions, increasing overall cost. 4. **Why not other options could be cheaper:** - A and C have small to moderate word totals. - D is larger than A and C but still far less than E and B. - B, while substantial, still has far fewer words than E, so E remains the higher-cost scenario. 5. **Conclusion:** - Among the given scenarios, embedding 500 long contracts totaling about 25 million words (E) would incur the highest cost for SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.EMBED_TEXT_768.

Question 24
Skipped
A team is building a RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) application. They plan to use CORTEX_SEARCH for the retrieval part. For the generation part, they want to use a fine-tuned model that specializes in their company's internal jargon. How should they reference this custom model in the COMPLETE function call that generates the final answer?
A. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('cortex-search', ...)
B. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('JOB_12345', ...) where JOB_12345 is the fine-tuning job ID.
Correct answer
C. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('my_finetuned_model', ...) where my_finetuned_model is the name given during fine-tuning.
D. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('COMPLETE', 'my_finetuned_model', ...)
E. They cannot use a fine-tuned model with a RAG application; they must use a base model.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('cortex-search', ...) - This option passes 'cortex-search' as the model name. If 'cortex-search' refers to a generic retrieval model, it may not represent the specific fine-tuned model you trained for your internal jargon. In Cortex, the COMPLETE function should reference the exact model you want to use for generation, which is typically the name of the deployed fine-tuned model. Merely using a generic or non-finetuned identifier could result in missing domain specialization. 2. B. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('JOB_12345', ...) where JOB_12345 is the fine-tuning job ID. - This is incorrect because the COMPLETE call should reference the deployed model, not the job ID from the fine-tuning process. The fine-tuning job ID is an internal reference and not the model identifier at runtime. The model you invoke must be the final fine-tuned model's name once deployed. 3. C. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('my_finetuned_model', ...) where my_finetuned_model is the name given during fine-tuning. - Correct. This uses the actual deployed model name (the one you specified during the fine-tuning process and deployed for inference). This ensures the generation uses your company’s internal jargon specialist model, which is essential for accurate, domain-specific responses in a RAG workflow. 4. D. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('COMPLETE', 'my_finetuned_model', ...) - This option references a FINETUNE operation, which is not how you invoke generation. FINETUNE is typically used to start or manage the fine-tuning process, not to perform the generation step in a RAG flow. For the generation step, you should use COMPLETE with the model name. 5. E. They cannot use a fine-tuned model with a RAG application; they must use a base model. - Incorrect. Snowflake Cortex supports using fine-tuned models for generation in RAG setups. The whole point of RAG with a tailored model is to improve relevance on your internal terminology. You can and should reference the fine-tuned model during COMPLETE. Conclusion Correct proposition: C. SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE('my_finetuned_model', ...) where my_finetuned_model is the name given during fine-tuning. This references the deployed, domain-specific model suitable for the final answer generation in your RAG workflow.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Snowflake Cortex - RAG with fine-tuned model

An administrator observes a sudden spike in credits consumed by Cortex functions. Which ACCOUNT_USAGE view will provide the quickest way to identify which specific function calls (COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT, etc.) and models are responsible for the spike?
A. QUERY_HISTORY
B. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY
Correct answer
C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY
D. METERING_DAILY_HISTORY
E. TASK_HISTORY
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. QUERY_HISTORY** - This view typically shows historical queries run in a data warehouse or system, including text, duration, and other query-related metrics. It does not specifically isolate Cortex function calls, their types (COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT, etc.), or the models used. Therefore, it’s not the quickest or most direct source for identifying spikes in Cortex function usage. 2. **B. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY** - This view reports metering data for warehouse usage (e.g., credits, storage, compute). While it can reveal overall credit spikes, it does not granularly break down Cortex function calls by type (COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT, etc.) or by which models were invoked. It’s useful for total cost signals but not for pinpointing the exact Cortex function usage drivers. 3. **C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY** - This view is designed to track usage of Cortex functions, including per-function call details (e.g., function name such as COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT) and the models used. It provides the quickest and most direct way to identify which specific function calls and models are responsible for a spike in Cortex usage. This is the targeted source for diagnosing the exact cause of increased Cortex credit consumption. 4. **D. METERING_DAILY_HISTORY** - This view aggregates daily metering data, potentially across various services. While it can show daily credit trends, it lacks the fine-grained granularity to tie spikes to particular Cortex functions or models. It’s less suitable for rapid pinpointing of the exact function calls causing the spike. 5. **E. TASK_HISTORY** - This view would cover scheduled or asynchronous tasks and their outcomes. It may provide some visibility into job-level activity but does not inherently break down Cortex function calls by type or model usage. It’s not the most direct path to identify Cortex-specific function call spikes. Correct Proposition **C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY** Detailed Explanation 1. **What the spike represents:** - A sudden increase in Cortex credits is usually driven by higher usage of Cortex functions (e.g., COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT) and/or calls to particular models. To diagnose quickly, you need visibility at the level of individual function calls and the models they invoked. 2. **Why CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY is best:** - It aggregates and surfaces per-function call data, including the function name and the model used. This lets an administrator filter or sort by function type and model, immediately revealing which combination is driving the spike. 3. **How it helps with quick identification:** - You can narrow down to a time window when the spike occurred and see the distribution of calls among COMP... functions (e.g., COMPARE, COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT) and corresponding models. This direct visibility accelerates pinpointing the root cause. 4. **Limitations of other views:** - QUERY_HISTORY: not Cortex-specific. - WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY and METERING_DAILY_HISTORY: give aggregated credit data, not per-function or per-model granularity. - TASK_HISTORY: focuses on tasks, not the granular Cortex function usage pattern. Conclusion To quickly identify which Cortex function calls and which models caused the spike in credits, use: **C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY**

Question 26
Skipped
When using Document AI, under which condition is it most appropriate to train a custom model rather than relying on the zero-shot capabilities of a pre-trained model?
A. When processing documents in a language not supported by the pre-trained models.
Correct answer
B. When the documents have a consistent, unique layout and terminology that differs significantly from the pre-trained model's training data.
C. When you need to extract only basic information like dates and names.
D. When you are processing a one-time batch of less than 100 documents.
E. When all the text in the documents is typed and not handwritten.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. When processing documents in a language not supported by the pre-trained models.** - This could be a reason to train a custom model, but many Document AI platforms offer multilingual pre-trained models or provide language adapters. Training a custom model specifically for an unsupported language is possible, but the more general and robust reason is when the domain/layout is specialized, not just language coverage. 2. **B. When the documents have a consistent, unique layout and terminology that differs significantly from the pre-trained model's training data.** - This is the strongest justification. A custom model shines when there is a stable, domain-specific structure (e.g., invoices from a particular vendor, healthcare forms, tax documents) and specialized terminology. The model can learn to reliably extract the exact fields in that layout, outperforming zero-shot extraction on unfamiliar formats. 3. **C. When you need to extract only basic information like dates and names.** - This is typically well-suited for zero-shot or simple rule-based extraction with generic pre-trained capabilities. A custom model is unlikely to offer a meaningful advantage for such simple, generic extraction tasks and may add unnecessary complexity. 4. **D. When you are processing a one-time batch of less than 100 documents.** - Training a custom model involves setup, labeled data, and iteration. For a tiny, one-off batch, a pre-trained model used in zero-shot mode is usually more efficient and cost-effective. Custom training isn’t justified for such small scale. 5. **E. When all the text in the documents is typed and not handwritten.** - Typed text generally behaves well with pre-trained models. Handwriting is where custom training often provides the biggest gains. If everything is typed, a custom model is unlikely to be necessary unless there is a very specialized extraction need beyond standard fields. ### Correct Proposition **B. When the documents have a consistent, unique layout and terminology that differs significantly from the pre-trained model's training data.** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Why a custom model helps with consistent, unique layouts:** - A custom model can learn the exact positions, labels, and structure of fields that recur across documents. - It can be tailored to recognize organization-specific forms, templates, and jargon, improving extraction accuracy and coverage. 2. **Role of domain-specific terminology:** - If the field names and values use specialized terms, a custom model can map these terms to standardized outputs, reducing misinterpretations that a generic model might make. 3. **Advantages over zero-shot in this scenario:** - Zero-shot extraction relies on broad, generic patterns and may miss or misinterpret fields in uncommon layouts. - A trained model with labeled examples can achieve higher precision and recall for the target documents. 4. **When to choose other options (to contrast):** - If language support is the only issue (A), explore multilingual pre-trained models or adapters first. - If the task is extremely simple (C) or scale is tiny (D), zero-shot or quick-rule approaches are better. - If documents are all typed (E), the benefit of training is reduced unless there is a substantial domain-specific extraction need. ### Conclusion Train a custom model when you have a stable, domain-specific set of documents with a unique layout and terminology that a pre-trained model cannot reliably handle. This yields higher accuracy and consistency for extraction tasks.

Question 27
Skipped
A developer wants to ensure that the output from SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE is always in Spanish, regardless of the input language. What is the most reliable method?
A. Set the temperature parameter to 0 to ensure deterministic output.
B. Use the TRANSLATE function on the output of the COMPLETE call.
Correct answer
C. Include a clear instruction in the prompt itself, e.g., "You must respond in Spanish.".
D. Use a fine-tuned model that was trained exclusively on Spanish text.
E. This is not possible; the model will always respond in the language of the input prompt.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Set the temperature parameter to 0 to ensure deterministic output.** - Este parámetro controla la aleatoriedad de las respuestas. Aunque una temperatura de 0 aumenta la previsibilidad, no garantiza que el modelo genere texto en español; podría seguir respondiendo en el idioma de la entrada si no se especifica explícitamente en el prompt o en la configuración. No es suficiente por sí solo para asegurar el idioma objetivo. 2. **B. Use the TRANSLATE function on the output of the COMPLETE call.** - Traducir la salida podría funcionar, pero añade una capa adicional y puede introducir errores de traducción o pérdida de matices. No garantiza que el contenido original esté en español, y complica el flujo de trabajo. 3. **C. Include a clear instruction in the prompt itself, e.g., "You must respond in Spanish."** - Esta es la forma más directa y confiable de forzar el idioma: especificar explícitamente en el prompt que la salida debe estar en español. Funciona consistentemente sin depender de configuraciones adicionales, modelos o etapas de procesamiento. 4. **D. Use a fine-tuned model that was trained exclusively on Spanish text.** - Un modelo fino de entrenamiento exclusivamente en español incrementa la probabilidad de respuestas en español, pero no garantiza al 100% si la entrada o el sistema de orquestación puede inyectar prompts o contextos en otros idiomas. Es más complejo y menos flexible que una instrucción explícita en el prompt. 5. **E. This is not possible; the model will always respond in the language of the input prompt.** - Falso. Es completamente posible forzar el idioma con instrucciones claras en el prompt (opción C). Esta afirmación subestima una técnica estándar de control de idioma. Propuesta correcta **C. Include a clear instruction in the prompt itself, e.g., "You must respond in Spanish."** Explicación detallada por proposition 1. A. Temperatura = 0 - Pros: mayor consistencia en la generación. - Contras: no garantiza el idioma; el modelo puede responder en el idioma de la entrada si no se especifica el idioma objetivo. No es una solución de control de idioma por sí misma. 2. B. Traducir la salida (TRANSLATE) - Pros: produce salida en español si la traducción es perfecta. - Contras: puede haber errores de traducción, pérdida de contexto o matices técnicos; añade complejidad y procesamiento extra. 3. C. Instrucción explícita en el prompt - Pros: método más directo y confiable. El modelo recibirá la indicación de responder en español y obedecerá. - Contras: requiere que la plataforma o capa que envía el prompt respete y conserve esa instrucción. 4. D. Modelo entrenado en español - Pros: mayor probabilidad de español, mejor manejo de matices. - Contras: no garantiza el idioma si hay instrucciones en otro idioma o si se redirige la salida. Mayor costo de mantenimiento y menos flexible ante cambios de requisitos. 5. E. No es posible - Contras: incorrecta; ya se demuestra que sí es posible mediante instrucciones de prompt (opción C) y otras técnicas. Conclusión La opción más fiable es: C. Include a clear instruction in the prompt itself, e.g., "You must respond in Spanish."

Question 28
Skipped
In the context of Cortex functions, what is the primary purpose of the max_tokens parameter in the COMPLETE function's options?
A. To limit the total number of function calls that can be made per minute to control costs.
B. To specify the maximum number of tokens from the input prompt that the model will process.
Correct answer
C. To define the maximum length of the generated output (completion) in tokens.
D. To set the total context window size (input + output) for the model.
E. To control the randomness of the output; a lower max_tokens makes the output more deterministic.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. To limit the total number of function calls that can be made per minute to control costs. - Incorrect. max_tokens has nothing to do with the rate of function calls. It controls how long the generated completion can be, not how many function invocations you can perform per minute. 2. B. To specify the maximum number of tokens from the input prompt that the model will process. - Incorrect. The input prompt tokens are generally fixed and provided as the prompt; max_tokens governs the length of the generated output, not how many input tokens are processed. The model processes the prompt as given, regardless of max_tokens. 3. C. To define the maximum length of the generated output (completion) in tokens. - Correct. max_tokens sets an upper bound on how many tokens the model is allowed to generate in the response. It controls output length, not input length or the total context window. 4. D. To set the total context window size (input + output) for the model. - Incorrect. The total context window (input tokens plus generated tokens) is determined by the model’s maximum context size. max_tokens only caps the output portion, not the entire context window. 5. E. To control the randomness of the output; a lower max_tokens makes the output more deterministic. - Incorrect. Randomness is controlled by parameters such as temperature or top_p. max_tokens does not influence randomness; it limits length. A low max_tokens simply truncates the output length, not its determinism. Correct Proposition C. To define the maximum length of the generated output (completion) in tokens. Detailed Explanation 1. Purpose of max_tokens: - It specifies how many tokens the model is allowed to generate after the prompt. This directly constrains the length of the completion. 2. Prompt vs. generation: - The prompt length is independent of max_tokens. The model will consider the prompt as provided and then generate up to max_tokens tokens in response. 3. Tokenization nuance: - Tokens are not the same as characters or words. The actual number of tokens generated may vary depending on the content and the tokenizer’s behavior, so the model might generate fewer than max_tokens if it finishes earlier or if constraints cause early termination. 4. Interaction with context window: - The model has a maximum context window (input plus output tokens). max_tokens cannot exceed this limit, and the effective output length may be reduced if the prompt is long. 5. Practical impact: - If you need a concise answer, use a smaller max_tokens. If you want a longer, more detailed response, set a larger max_tokens (within the model’s context limits). Note that extremely large values may lead to longer processing times and higher cost. Conclusion In the Cortex functions context, the primary purpose of max_tokens in the COMPLETE function’s options is to define the maximum length of the generated output in tokens (Option C).
Domain
Knowledge Area: Output token limits (generated length)
Question 29
Skipped
A company uses a Cortex function within a materialized view to enrich data daily. They notice the view takes a long time to refresh. What is a potential strategy to improve refresh performance?
A. Replace the materialized view with a standard view.
Correct answer
B. Move the Cortex function call to a separate table that is updated incrementally and then join to that table.
C. Increase the max_tokens parameter of the Cortex function to get more complete answers faster.
D. Use a larger virtual warehouse for the materialized view refresh.
E. Change the Cortex function to a JavaScript UDF.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Replace the materialized view with a standard view - Evaluation: Not a good strategy. A standard view is computed on every query, not precomputed. The refresh burden disappears, but you lose the performance benefits of precomputation, and for a daily refresh you’d still incur the cost of enriching data on demand. This does not address the heavy Cortex enrichment work during refresh. 2. B. Move the Cortex function call to a separate table that is updated incrementally and then join to that table - Evaluation: Strong candidate. By decoupling the Cortex enrichment from the daily refresh and updating an incremental enrichment table, you avoid re-running the Cortex function for every existing row. The incremental process handles only new or changed data, reducing compute and external calls, and the final materialized view can simply join to the precomputed enrichment. This often yields a substantial refresh-time improvement. 3. C. Increase the max_tokens parameter of the Cortex function to get more complete answers faster - Evaluation: Likely counterproductive. Increasing max_tokens increases the amount of processing and response time for each Cortex invocation, not speed. This would generally worsen refresh duration rather than improve it. 4. D. Use a larger virtual warehouse for the materialized view refresh - Evaluation: Potentially helpful but situational. A larger warehouse can speed up the compute-heavy refresh, but since the Cortex enrichment is the main bottleneck, the improvement is indirect and may be cost-inefficient. It may help if the refresh is compute-bound, but it doesn’t address the root cause of re-running Cortex for many rows. 5. E. Change the Cortex function to a JavaScript UDF - Evaluation: Not advisable. JavaScript UDFs run inside the data warehouse and may not offer the same performance or scalability as the Cortex service. In many cases, this would shift or worsen the bottleneck rather than alleviate it. Correct Proposition B. Move the Cortex function call to a separate table that is updated incrementally and then join to that table Detailed Explanation - Why Cortex enrichment is costly during refresh: - A materialized view refresh re-executes the underlying query, which in this case includes calling the Cortex function for enrichment. If enrichment is compute- or I/O-intensive, this dominates refresh time. - Incremental enrichment approach: - Maintain a separate enrichment table that stores Cortex-enabled results for data up to the last successful refresh. - On each daily run, compute enrichment only for new or changed rows (incremental load), update the enrichment table, and then refresh the MV by joining to this precomputed enrichment table. - Benefits: - Reduces redundant Cortex calls on unchanged data. - Shortens overall refresh time by limiting Cortex invocations to incremental data. - Simplifies error handling: partial refreshes can be retried without reprocessing the entire dataset. - Improves cache and I/O efficiency because the enrichment table is smaller and more predictable. - Practical considerations: - Ensure proper handling of late-arriving data and data versioning. - Use appropriate decay or reprocessing rules if enrichment logic changes. - Index or partition the enrichment table to optimize incremental reads. Conclusion To improve refresh performance when a Cortex function enriches data in a daily materialized view, the most effective strategy is to decouple and incrementally update the Cortex-enriched data in a separate table and join it during the MV refresh. This reduces redundant Cortex processing and accelerates the refresh process. Therefore, option B is the recommended choice.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Materialized view refresh performance optimi
Question 30
Skipped
For a Cortex Search service, what does the TARGET_LAG parameter primarily control?
A. The maximum acceptable latency for a single search query.
Correct answer
B. The freshness of the search index compared to the source table (i.e., how quickly updates are reflected).
C. The geographical lag between the source data and the search index for global replication.
D. The computational lag of the underlying warehouse processing the search queries.
E. The rate-limiting lag for controlling the number of queries per second.
Overall explanation
Propositions Analysis for Cortex Search: TARGET_LAG 1. A. The maximum acceptable latency for a single search query - This is not what TARGET_LAG controls. TARGET_LAG deals with data freshness relative to the source, not the runtime latency of executing a query. It’s about how up-to-date the index is, not how long a query takes to run. 2. B. The freshness of the search index compared to the source table (i.e., how quickly updates are reflected) - This is correct. TARGET_LAG specifies how far behind the search index may be from the source data. It governs data freshness by controlling the allowed lag between when data changes in the source and when those changes are visible in the index used for search. A smaller TARGET_LAG means fresher results but may require more frequent/rigorous indexing or streaming so that updates propagate quickly. 3. C. The geographical lag between the source data and the search index for global replication - This is not accurate. TARGET_LAG is about time-based staleness, not geographic latency. It doesn’t measure or enforce replication delays across regions intentionally, though in multi-region setups the effective lag could be impacted by replication topology, it is not the defined purpose of TARGET_LAG. 4. D. The computational lag of the underlying warehouse processing the search queries - This is incorrect. TARGET_LAG is not about how long the warehouse spends computing results during query execution. It relates to data synchronization latency between source and index, not query processing time or compute overhead during queries. 5. E. The rate-limiting lag for controlling the number of queries per second - This is not what TARGET_LAG controls. Rate limiting is a separate concern related to throughput and request pacing, whereas TARGET_LAG is about data freshness between source data and the indexed data used for search. Correct Proposition - The most accurate option is B: The freshness of the search index compared to the source table (i.e., how quickly updates are reflected). Detailed Explanation - What TARGET_LAG controls: - TARGET_LAG defines the maximum acceptable time difference between when a data change occurs in the source (e.g., a table) and when that change is visible in the search index used by Cortex Search. - It directly impacts data freshness: a lower TARGET_LAG yields fresher search results because updates propagate to the index more promptly; a higher TARGET_LAG may reduce indexing load or resource usage but at the cost of staler results. - Why freshness matters: - In use cases where real-time or near-real-time visibility of data is important (e.g., monitoring, alerting, recent transactions), keeping the index up-to-date is critical for accurate search outcomes. - How it differs from other aspects: - It is not about per-query latency (A), geographic replication timing (C), processing time for queries (D), or enforcing a query per-second cap (E). Those represent runtime performance, distribution architecture, or throughput controls, not index freshness. - Practical impact: - Operators set TARGET_LAG according to acceptable staleness versus resource cost. A very aggressive (low) TARGET_LAG can increase indexing load, require more frequent ingestion pipelines, and potentially higher operational complexity, whereas a lenient (high) TARGET_LAG can reduce load but deliver older results.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Data freshness (indexing latency) in Cortex
Question 31
Skipped
A company is using Cortex Search to power a knowledge base. They notice that queries containing specific product codes (e.g., "PRD-8472") are not returning the most relevant documents, even though those codes exist in the source tables. What is the most likely explanation and solution?
A. The model used by Cortex Search is not trained on alphanumeric codes. They should fine-tune the model on their product codes.
Correct answer
B. The semantic search is interpreting "PRD-8472" as a word rather than a unique identifier. They should augment semantic search with a traditional keyword search filter.
C. The TARGET_LAG is set too high, causing the index to be stale. They should decrease the TARGET_LAG value.
D. The source table has row-level security enabled, which is blocking index updates. They should disable RLS for the indexed columns.
E. The embedding model used (e5-base-v2) cannot handle numeric values. They should switch to a different embedding model.
Overall explanation
Correct proposition: B Detailed explanations for each option 1. A. The model used by Cortex Search is not trained on alphanumeric codes. They should fine-tune the model on their product codes. - Why this is unlikely: Cortex Search’s embedding model (e.g., e5-base-v2) is designed to work with a wide range of text, including codes and identifiers. The issue described (codes existing in source tables but not surfacing in results) is typically not due to the model’s training on codes, but how the search is composed and indexed. Fine-tuning on codes is a heavier, specialized step and not usually necessary to surface exact identifiers in a semantic search context. 2. B. The semantic search is interpreting "PRD-8472" as a word rather than a unique identifier. They should augment semantic search with a traditional keyword search filter. - Why this is correct: Semantic (vector) search excels at natural language queries but can struggle with precise, short identifiers like product codes, which are better served by exact-match keyword matching. If the code exists in the index but is not being prioritized, adding a keyword/filters-based constraint or using a keyword field for the code (and filtering on it) ensures exact matches surface as expected. This combination—vector search for context plus keyword search for identifiers—often yields the most relevant results for product codes. - How to implement: index the product code as a keyword/not-analyzed field, and apply a filter (e.g., code:"PRD-8472") or boost exact matches in the query alongside the semantic component. 3. C. The TARGET_LAG is set too high, causing the index to be stale. They should decrease the TARGET_LAG value. - Why this is unlikely: TARGET_LAG relates to index freshness in some data pipelines, but the description says the codes exist in source tables, implying the data is present in the index. If the index were stale, you’d typically miss newer documents rather than fail to surface existing codes. The problem described points to retrieval quality rather than data freshness. 4. D. The source table has row-level security enabled, which is blocking index updates. They should disable RLS for the indexed columns. - Why this is unlikely: If RLS were blocking indexing, you wouldn’t see documents authored by users with insufficient privileges, and the code would be missing or inaccessible, not present in source tables. The symptoms described (codes exist but not returned as relevant) don’t match RLS misconfiguration as the primary cause. Additionally, RLS typically affects access at query time, not the relevance ranking of documents already indexed. 5. E. The embedding model used (e5-base-v2) cannot handle numeric values. They should switch to a different embedding model. - Why this is unlikely: e5-base-v2 (and similar multilingual/encoder models) can handle alphanumeric tokens, including numbers, within queries and documents. The issue is not usually the model’s incapacity to handle numbers but how the numeric code is represented and queried. Switching models is a broader change that isn’t necessary when the root cause is the missing keyword/identifier-specific filtering. Conclusion The most likely explanation is B: semantic search alone misinterprets the product code as a general term rather than a precise identifier. Augment semantic search with a keyword/filters-based approach (index the product code as a keyword field and apply exact-match filtering or boosting) to ensure PRD-8472 surfaces the most relevant documents.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Semantic search and relevance (retrieval/ran
Question 32
Skipped
A developer is using the SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE function with a detailed, 5-shot learning prompt. They find that for very long prompts, the function sometimes returns an error. What is the most probable cause and the correct mitigation strategy?
A. The error is due to rate limiting. The solution is to add a delay between consecutive function calls.
Correct answer
B. The prompt exceeds the model's context window limit. The solution is to chunk the prompt or reduce the number of examples.
C. The model is unable to process examples in the prompt. The solution is to remove all examples and use zero-shot learning.
D. The output is being truncated. The solution is to increase the max_tokens parameter.
E. There is a syntax error in the prompt's JSON structure. The solution is to validate the prompt format.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. The error is due to rate limiting. The solution is to add a delay between consecutive function calls. - Rate limiting could cause failures, but it usually results in a retryable error rather than a failure caused specifically by very long prompts. Rate limiting is more about how many requests per time unit you send, not about prompt length. Adding delays helps only if you’re exceeding API call quotas, not if the prompt itself is too large. This is unlikely to be the most probable cause in the scenario described. 2. B. The prompt exceeds the model's context window limit. The solution is to chunk the prompt or reduce the number of examples. - This is the most probable cause. Very long prompts, especially with a 5-shot learning setup, can exceed the model’s maximum context length. When the prompt plus expected output cannot fit in the model’s context, the API may return an error. The valid mitigation is to shorten the prompt (fewer shots, shorter examples) or split the task into smaller prompts and compose results in post-processing. 3. C. The model is unable to process examples in the prompt. The solution is to remove all examples and use zero-shot learning. - While removing examples to use zero-shot could avoid context-length issues, it isn’t addressing the root cause if the prompt is still too long. Moreover, removing all examples may degrade performance and accuracy. It’s an extreme measure and not the most appropriate first step when the prompt is lengthy but still usable with proper chunking. 4. D. The output is being truncated. The solution is to increase the max_tokens parameter. - If output truncation were the primary issue, increasing max_tokens would help. However, the prompt length causing the error (not enough context to process the request) is separate from the length of the output. In many cases, long prompts fail before generating any meaningful output, so simply increasing max_tokens may not resolve the root cause. 5. E. There is a syntax error in the prompt's JSON structure. The solution is to validate the prompt format. - A JSON syntax error would typically cause an immediate parsing failure before any model processing. While it's important to ensure valid JSON, such errors are usually detectable at prompt construction time. The described problem (“very long prompts” causing an error) aligns more with context length limits than with JSON syntax issues. Correct Proposition B. The prompt exceeds the model's context window limit. The solution is to chunk the prompt or reduce the number of examples. Detailed Explanation - Context window limit: Modern large language models have a maximum number of tokens they can attend to in a single input (prompt + expected output). Very long prompts, especially with multiple-shot examples, can easily surpass this limit, leading to errors or incomplete processing. - 5-shot prompt: Each example adds tokens. With long instructions and multiple examples, you can quickly exhaust the context window, leaving insufficient space for the model to generate a proper output. - Practical mitigations: - Reduce the number of shots (e.g., switch from 5-shot to 2- or 3-shot) or shorten each example. - Chunk the prompt into smaller segments and process them separately, then aggregate results. - Use concise, essential examples only, or split the task into multiple prompts that each stay within the context limit. - If possible, summarize or compress the examples to convey the same information with fewer tokens. - Why not other options: - Rate limiting (A) is about throughput, not prompt length, and would show different error patterns. - Removing all examples (C) is a drastic measure and not a targeted solution to context length issues. - Increasing max_tokens (D) addresses output length, not context-length errors. - JSON syntax errors (E) would prevent parsing earlier than any long-prompt issue and would be evident as a formatting problem.
Domain
Knowledge area: Large language models — prompt design and co
Question 33
Skipped
When comparing the cost structure of SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.SUMMARIZE versus using SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE with a custom prompt for summarization, which statement is accurate?
A. SUMMARIZE is always more expensive because it uses a larger, more specialized model.
B. COMPLETE is always cheaper because it's more general-purpose.
Correct answer
C. SUMMARIZE may be more cost-effective as it's optimized for the task and might consume fewer output tokens for the same quality.
D. The cost is identical because both functions use the same underlying model.
E. SUMMARIZE has a fixed cost per call, while COMPLETE costs vary by token, making SUMMARIZE cheaper for long documents.
Overall explanation
Option-by-option analysis 1. A. SUMMARIZE is always more expensive because it uses a larger, more specialized model. - Incorrect. A more specialized model can be more cost-efficient for a given task because it is tuned for that task, potentially delivering the same quality with fewer tokens or processing steps. “Always more expensive” is not supported. 2. B. COMPLETE is always cheaper because it's more general-purpose. - Incorrect. Being more general-purpose does not guarantee lower cost. COST depends on token usage and model behavior; a general model could generate more tokens to reach the same quality, increasing cost. “Always cheaper” is not accurate. 3. C. SUMMARIZE may be more cost-effective as it's optimized for the task and might consume fewer output tokens for the same quality. - Correct. A task-optimized summarization function can reduce both input and output tokens required to achieve the same quality, lowering cost relative to a generic completion with a custom prompt. This aligns with typical cost-saving benefits of task-specific tooling. 4. D. The cost is identical because both functions use the same underlying model. - Incorrect. Even if underlying capabilities are related, the wrapper function and prompt design can yield different token counts and performance, leading to different costs. “Identical cost” is not guaranteed. 5. E. SUMMARIZE has a fixed cost per call, while COMPLETE costs vary by token, making SUMMARIZE cheaper for long documents. - Incorrect. In most token-based pricing models, costs are token-based for both calls (input and output tokens) and do not have a strictly fixed per-call cost. The statement about a fixed cost for SUMMARIZE is not generally true, and for long documents, the token-based nature means costs scale with token usage in both options. Conclusion - Correct proposition: C. SUMMARIZE may be more cost-effective as it's optimized for the task and might consume fewer output tokens for the same quality.

Question 34
Skipped
An administrator wants to ensure that only specific LLM models (e.g., llama3.1-8b and mistral-7b) can be used in their Snowflake account to control costs and compliance. How can they achieve this?
A. Revoke the USAGE privilege on all other models from the PUBLIC role.
Correct answer
B. Use the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter to specify the allowed models.
C. Create a resource monitor that blocks queries using non-approved models.
D. Implement a stored procedure that validates the model name before executing any COMPLETE call.
E. This is not possible; all available models in a region can be used by any user with the necessary privileges.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Revoke the USAGE privilege on all other models from the PUBLIC role.** - Why it’s not ideal: Revoke from PUBLIC on every non-approved model could be operationally heavy and fragile. It also relies on the PUBLIC role having broad implicit access to models by default, and you’d need to manage grants precisely for all models, including newly created ones. It doesn’t provide a centralized, scalable allowlist mechanism and can lead to gaps or accidental blockages if new models are introduced. - Also: You’d still need to explicitly grant USAGE on the allowed models to the appropriate roles. This approach is manual and error-prone and not as clean as a dedicated allowlist mechanism. 2. **B. Use the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter to specify the allowed models.** - Why it’s the correct approach: This option leverages a built-in account parameter designed to restrict which Cortex (LLM) models can be used within the Snowflake account. By specifying only the allowed models (e.g., llama3.1-8b and mistral-7b), you enforce an allowlist at the account level, covering all users and sessions without requiring changes to each user’s grants or app code. - Key advantage: Centralized control, scalable, and enforceable across all workloads that utilize Cortex models. It reduces risk of unauthorized model use and helps with cost and compliance. 3. **C. Create a resource monitor that blocks queries using non-approved models.** - Why it’s not appropriate: Resource monitors in Snowflake control credit consumption and alert or suspend based on usage thresholds, not on the content or parameters of a query. They cannot selectively block queries based on which Cortex model is invoked. This approach won’t reliably enforce an allowlist for models. 4. **D. Implement a stored procedure that validates the model name before executing any COMPLETE call.** - Why it’s insufficient alone: A stored procedure can enforce model validation within its own logic, but it cannot guarantee enforcement across all queries and clients unless every call to a Cortex model goes through that procedure. This would require a significant change to application code and query patterns, and could be bypassed if users call the models directly or via other execution paths. It’s a workaround rather than a robust, account-wide policy. 5. **E. This is not possible; all available models in a region can be used by any user with the necessary privileges.** - Why it’s incorrect: Snowflake provides governance controls (including account parameters and access controls) to restrict model usage. Claiming it’s not possible ignores the available mechanism (the Cortex models allowlist parameter) and would be factually incorrect for standard Cortex usage. Correct Proposition **B. Use the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter to specify the allowed models.** Detailed implications - How it works: Set the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter to the list of permitted model identifiers (e.g., llama3.1-8b, mistral-7b). Snowflake will then restrict Cortex-based model usage to only those models across all users and sessions within the account. - Benefits: - Centralized governance: One place to manage allowed models. - Consistent enforcement: All workloads adhere to the allowlist automatically. - Cost and compliance: Prevents inadvertent usage of disallowed models, helping control spend and ensure policy compliance. - Practical steps (high level): - Identify the exact Cortex model identifiers you want to allow. - Set the account parameter, e.g., ALTER ACCOUNT SET CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST=['llama3.1-8b','mistral-7b']; - Validate by running a test query that attempts to use an allowed model and one that would be disallowed (to confirm enforcement). - Monitor for any anomalies and adjust the allowlist as needed. Conclusion Use option B to enforce a robust, scalable allowlist of Cortex models across the Snowflake account.
