Question 1: Document AI & Mixed Content Scenario: Your company is automating contract processing using Snowflake Document AI. The contracts contain standard typed clauses but also include handwritten signatures and occasional handwritten notes in the margins. Challenge: What is the most accurate expectation for how Document AI will process these documents, and what is a critical consideration for the production system?
A. Document AI will automatically ignore all handwritten content, focusing only on typed text for higher accuracy.
Correct answer
B. Document AI will process both typed and handwritten text, but the extraction accuracy for handwritten content will likely be lower and require a validation workflow.
C. Handwritten content requires a separate, specialized AI model outside of Document AI and must be pre-processed.
D. Documents containing any handwritten elements cannot be processed by Document AI and will be rejected.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Document AI will automatically ignore all handwritten content, focusing only on typed text for higher accuracy.** - Not correct. Document AI does not deliberately ignore handwritten content. It attempts to extract whatever is legible and detectable, including handwriting, but handwriting typically has lower recognition accuracy than typed text. Expectation of ignoring handwriting is inaccurate. 2. **B. Document AI will process both typed and handwritten text, but the extraction accuracy for handwritten content will likely be lower and require a validation workflow.** - Correct. Document AI can process both typed and handwritten content. Handwritten text generally yields lower accuracy than typed text, and for production use you typically implement a validation/verification step (e.g., human-in-the-loop or automated confidence checks) to ensure critical clauses or signatures are correctly captured. 3. **C. Handwritten content requires a separate, specialized AI model outside of Document AI and must be pre-processed.** - Not correct. While there are specialized handwriting models in general, in the context of Document AI, handwriting can be handled within the same platform with appropriate models and configurations. External pre-processing is not a strict requirement. 4. **D. Documents containing any handwritten elements cannot be processed by Document AI and will be rejected.** - Not correct. Document AI is designed to handle mixed content, including handwritten elements. Rejection based solely on handwriting is not accurate. Correct Proposition **B. Document AI will process both typed and handwritten text, but the extraction accuracy for handwritten content will likely be lower and require a validation workflow** Detailed Explanation 1. **What to expect with typed vs. handwritten text:** - Typed text is highly accurate for OCR/NER extraction because fonts are consistent and well-structured. - Handwritten text introduces variability (slower handwriting, cursive, non-standard characters) that reduces recognition accuracy. 2. **Impact on a production system:** - For contracts, key fields (dates, party names, signatures) may appear in handwritten notes or margins. Expect higher uncertainty on these elements. - A validation workflow (automatic confidence scoring, exception queues, or human review) helps maintain overall document quality and process reliability. 3. **Practical production considerations:** - Implement confidence thresholds: fields with low confidence get routed to validation. - Use post-processing rules to cross-verify critical clauses against standard templates. - Maintain an audit trail of manual reviews for compliance and quality assurance. - Consider human-in-the-loop for signature areas if legal enforceability hinges on accuracy. 4. **Why not the other options:** - A dismisses handwritten content entirely, which is contrary to Document AI’s capabilities and would reduce usable information. - C implies a separate external model is mandatory, which is not true for the typical use of Document AI; it can handle handwriting with built-in/compatible models. - D incorrectly asserts universal rejection of documents with handwriting, which would be unnecessarily restrictive and impractical. Conclusion The most accurate expectation is that Document AI will process both typed and handwritten text, but handwritten extraction will be less accurate and should be supported by a validation/human-in-the-loop workflow in production.

Question 2: Fine-Tuning Foundation Models Scenario: Your data science team has curated a dataset of 1,000 high-quality prompt-completion pairs to adapt a general-purpose LLM for your company's specific legal jargon. Challenge: Which SQL command correctly initiates a fine-tuning job in Snowflake Cortex to create this custom model?
Correct answer
A. CALL FINE_TUNE_MODEL('legal_llm', 'training_table', {'epochs': 5});
B. CREATE MODEL legal_llm USING FINETUNE('mistral-7b', 'training_table');
C. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('CREATE', 'mistral-7b', 'training_table', {'name': 'legal_llm'});
D. ALTER MODEL mistral-7b ADD FINE_TUNING DATA FROM training_table;
Overall explanation
Option analysis 1. A. CALL FINE_TUNE_MODEL('legal_llm', 'training_table', {'epochs': 5}); - Likely correct. The syntax resembles a stored procedure call to initiate a fine-tuning job, using: - 'legal_llm' as the target model name to create or update. - 'training_table' as the source table containing the 1,000 prompt-completion pairs. - A parameter object {'epochs': 5} to specify training hyperparameters. - Pros: - Uses a procedural CALL, which is a common pattern for starting long-running jobs in Snowflake. - Explicitly creates or updates a model named 'legal_llm' with training data from a table. - Cons: - Requires that a stored procedure FINE_TUNE_MODEL exists with that exact signature. If the actual Cortex API uses a different procedure name or parameter structure, this would fail. 2. B. CREATE MODEL legal_llm USING FINETUNE('mistral-7b', 'training_table'); - Potentially plausible if Cortex exposes a model creation path tied to finetuning and if FINETUNE is a valid function. - Pros: - Clear intention: create a new model named 'legal_llm' using a finetune process on base model 'mistral-7b'. - Cons: - The syntax looks atypical for Snowflake Cortex, which usually separates model creation from finetuning configuration. Real-world implementations often require a dedicated finetune function/stored procedure rather than embedding in CREATE MODEL syntax. - Uses 'training_table' directly without an explicit parameter for epochs or other hyperparameters, which may be insufficient or invalid. 3. C. SELECT SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.FINETUNE('CREATE', 'mistral-7b', 'training_table', {'name': 'legal_llm'}); - This is a more explicit, fully-qualified call pattern, but the exact arguments ('CREATE', base model, training source, options) may not reflect the actual API. - Pros: - Clearly indicates a finetune operation within the Snowflake Cortex namespace. - Option to name the new model via {'name': 'legal_llm'}. - Cons: - The first argument 'CREATE' and the overall function FINETUNE may not match Snowflake Cortex’s real function names, signatures, or return semantics. - Using SELECT to invoke a finetune operation is unusual if Cortex finetuning is implemented as a callable procedure or job; SELECT is typically for querying results, not for starting jobs. 4. D. ALTER MODEL mistral-7b ADD FINE_TUNING DATA FROM training_table; - Conceptually reasonable (altering an existing base model with additional tuning data), but: - Pros: - Mirrors a workflow where you augment a base model with additional data. - Cons: - It attempts to modify an existing base model ('mistral-7b') rather than creating a new specialized model ('legal_llm'). - The syntax is speculative and may not align with how finetuning data is provided or how Cortex applies updates (often you specify a new target model name, not in-place alteration of a base model). - If the intent is to produce a new fine-tuned model, this option likely does not accomplish that. Correct proposition The most suitable option for initiating a fine-tuning job to create the custom model is: A. CALL FINE_TUNE_MODEL('legal_llm', 'training_table', {'epochs': 5}); Detailed explanation 1. How fine-tuning is typically initiated: - Fine-tuning a large language model generally requires: - Selecting a base model (e.g., mistral-7b). - Providing a dataset (training_table) with prompt–completion pairs. - Supplying hyperparameters (epochs, learning rate, batch size, etc.). - Requesting the creation of a new, named fine-tuned model (here, legal_llm). - A stored procedure or dedicated API call that encapsulates these steps is common, because the operation is long-running and resource-intensive. 2. Why A matches this pattern: - It uses a CALL to trigger a job (typical for long-running operations in Snowflake). - The signature implies: - Target model name: 'legal_llm' (the result is a fine-tuned model with this name). - Training source: 'training_table' (the table holding the 1,000 prompt–completion pairs). - Hyperparameters: {'epochs': 5} (configurable training parameter). - This aligns with the expectation of a managed, explicit finetuning workflow that produces a distinct model artifact rather than mutating a base model in place. 3. Why the other options are less suitable: - B may be syntactically plausible in some systems, but it combines creation and finetuning in a single CREATE MODEL statement with an unusual FINETUNE syntax, which may not exist in Snowflake Cortex’s actual API. - C could work if the exact function and execution route exist, but the use of SELECT to start a finetune is atypical; startups of long-running jobs are usually done via CALL or a dedicated API, not via SELECT. - D implies altering the base model directly, which would not yield a separate, named fine-tuned model (and most platforms prefer creating a new model from fine-tuning rather than mutating the base). Conclusion Option A is the most appropriate choice to initiate a fine-tuning job and produce a new model named legal_llm using the training data from training_table with a defined hyperparameter (epochs). If your environment uses different exact function names or signatures, adapt to the documented Cortex finetuning procedure, but the conceptual approach remains: start a dedicated finetuning job via a callable/procedure with a target model name, training data reference, and hyperparameters.

Question 3: Generating Structured Data from LLMs Scenario: You are building an API that uses an LLM to extract customer information from support tickets. The downstream system requires the data in a strict, predefined JSON schema (e.g., {"name": "...", "issue": "...", "priority": "..."}). Challenge: Which Cortex capability ensures the LLM's output conforms exactly to this schema without needing additional parsing logic?
A. Use EXTRACT_ANSWER for each field and manually assemble the JSON object.
B. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize the ticket and then construct JSON based on the category.
C. Use the standard COMPLETE function with a prompt that explicitly asks for JSON, and then validate the output.
Correct answer
D. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by providing the required JSON schema in the function parameters.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Use EXTRACT_ANSWER for each field and manually assemble the JSON object.** - This approach relies on separate extraction steps for each field and then manual assembly of JSON. - It introduces potential inconsistencies and parsing errors if any field extraction diverges from the schema. - It requires additional logic outside the LLM to enforce the exact JSON structure, defeating the goal of avoiding extra parsing logic. 2. **B. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to categorize the ticket and then construct JSON based on the category.** - Classify_text is intended for categorizing input (e.g., topic, severity) rather than extracting structured, field-level data. - After classification, you’d still need separate steps to populate a strict JSON schema, which implies extra parsing/assembly logic. - This option does not directly guarantee the LLM’s output conforms to a fixed JSON schema. 3. **C. Use the standard COMPLETE function with a prompt that explicitly asks for JSON, and then validate the output.** - Asking for JSON in the prompt can help, but “validate the output” implies client-side or post-processing validation to ensure the exact schema. - Validation is a parsing/verification step, not a built-in guarantee that the LLM output strictly adheres to the schema without extra logic. - It still relies on free-form output that must be parsed and validated against the schema. 4. **D. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by providing the required JSON schema in the function parameters.** - This option leverages a specialized capability designed for structured outputs. - By supplying the exact JSON schema in the function parameters, the LLM is guided to produce output that matches the predefined schema without needing additional parsing logic. - It directly enforces the strict, machine-consumable JSON shape required by the downstream system. Correct Proposition **D. Use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by providing the required JSON schema in the function parameters.** Detailed Explanation - The core requirement is to have the LLM output conform exactly to a predefined JSON schema (e.g., {"name": "...", "issue": "...", "priority": "..."}), with no need for downstream parsing or heavy post-processing. - Structured Outputs capabilities in Cortex are specifically designed to yield outputs that adhere to a given schema, minimizing the gap between the LLM’s generation and the downstream data format. - By providing the required JSON schema in the function parameters, you constrain the model to produce a compliant object, reducing or eliminating the need for extra parsing, validation, or field-by-field extraction steps. - The alternative options (A, B, C) either split extraction into multiple steps, rely on classification, or depend on post-generation validation, all of which introduce additional parsing or handling to enforce the schema. Conclusion To guarantee that the LLM’s output conforms exactly to the predefined JSON schema with minimal extra processing, use AI_COMPLETE Structured Outputs by supplying the exact JSON schema in the function parameters. This directly enforces the desired structure and is the most efficient approach for downstream systems requiring strict JSON data.

Knowledge Area: Structured Outputs

Question 4: Optimizing Context-Aware Processing Scenario: You are using Cortex functions to analyze a diverse set of long documents (e.g., legal contracts, technical reports, and marketing emails). You want to maximize the quality and consistency of the results. Challenge: Which THREE factors will have the most significant positive impact on your results?
A. Using the exact same prompt template for all document types to ensure uniformity.
Correct selection
B. Providing 2-3 relevant input-output examples within your prompts (few-shot learning).
C. Always using the largest, most expensive available model for every task.
Correct selection
D. Intelligently chunking large documents to fit within the model's context window.
Correct selection
E. Setting a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for extraction tasks and a higher one (e.g., 0.7) for creative tasks.
Overall explanation
Option Analysis 1. A. Using the exact same prompt template for all document types to ensure uniformity - Why this is not ideal: Different document types (legal contracts, technical reports, marketing emails) have distinct goals, terminology, structures, and required outputs. A single template may fail to elicit the most relevant information from each type and can lead to lower quality results. - Practical impact: Uniform prompts reduce the model’s ability to tailor guidance, extraction, or summaries to the nuances of each document category. You may miss document-specific cues or misinterpret domain-specific language. - Bottom line: Not a positive factor for maximizing quality and consistency across varied long documents. 2. B. Providing 2-3 relevant input-output examples within your prompts (few-shot learning) - Why this helps: Few-shot examples show the model how you expect it to format outputs, handle domain-specific tasks, and align with your evaluation criteria. It guides the model toward the desired style, level of detail, and type of information to extract. - Practical impact: Improves consistency and accuracy across diverse documents by providing concrete exemplars for the model to imitate, reducing ambiguity in unstructured prompts. - Bottom line: A strong positive factor for improving results, especially when you have varied tasks across document types. 3. C. Always using the largest, most expensive available model for every task - Why this is not ideal: The largest models offer higher capacity but come with higher latency, cost, and potential for diminishing returns on straightforward tasks. For many extraction, summarization, or classification tasks, a smaller or task-tuned model can perform just as well or better, especially when prompts are well-designed. - Practical impact: Increases operational costs and may not yield proportional gains. Could slow workflows and reduce scalability when processing many documents. - Bottom line: Not a reliable positive factor; not recommended as a universal approach. 4. D. Intelligently chunking large documents to fit within the model's context window - Why this matters: Large documents often exceed a model’s token context window. Intelligent chunking preserves important context, maintains logical boundaries (e.g., sections, clauses), and enables incremental processing without losing critical information. - Practical impact: Improves accuracy of extractions, summaries, and analysis by ensuring the model has the most relevant surrounding content for each chunk. You can still aggregate results across chunks to form a coherent overall output. - Bottom line: A strong positive factor for maximizing quality and consistency. 5. E. Setting a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for extraction tasks and a higher one (e.g., 0.7) for creative tasks - Why this helps: Temperature controls randomness. Extraction and factual tasks benefit from determinism to minimize hallucinations and maintain precision, while creative tasks benefit from variability to generate richer, more diverse outputs. - Practical impact: Tailors the model's behavior to the task, improving reliability for structured outputs (low temperature) and creativity when appropriate (higher temperature). This supports both accuracy and usefulness across document types. - Bottom line: A strong positive factor when task-appropriate temperature is applied. Correct propositions and rationale - The three factors that will have the most significant positive impact: B, D, and E. - B (few-shot examples) provides clear guidance and reduces ambiguity across document types. - D (intelligent chunking) ensures the model can process long documents within context limits while preserving coherence. - E (task-appropriate temperature) optimizes output style for extraction versus creative tasks. Conclusion For optimizing context-aware processing of long documents, use few-shot demonstrations (B), implement intelligent chunking to fit context windows (D), and adjust temperature by task (E). A and C are not beneficial in this scenario.
Domain
Knowledge area: Prompting and context management

B. Python is the primary interface, requiring Snowpark; SQL and REST are not available.
Correct answer
C. Cortex functions are accessible via SQL, Python (through Snowpark), and REST APIs, enabling integration across all these contexts.
D. All calls must be routed through a dedicated REST API gateway, even those from within Snowflake.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Cortex functions can only be called from within Snowflake SQL queries.** - This is too restrictive. While SQL access is one mode, it ignores other viable interfaces that teams may use (Python, REST). 2. **B. Python is the primary interface, requiring Snowpark; SQL and REST are not available.** - This is incorrect. It wrongly elevates Python as the sole interface and incorrectly excludes SQL and REST access. 3. **C. Final answer: C is correct. Cortex functions are accessible via SQL, Python (through Snowpark), and REST APIs, enabling integration across all these contexts.

Question 6: Implementing Safety Guardrails Scenario: You are developing a customer-facing chatbot using SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE. To comply with company policy, you must prevent the model from generating harmful, biased, or inappropriate content and from leaking PII. Challenge: How do you activate the built-in Cortex Guard safety features for your function calls?
A. Install and configure the separate "Cortex Guard" extension in your Snowflake account.
B. Set the account-level parameter CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED = TRUE to apply guardrails globally.
C. Create a separate CORTEX_GUARD service and route all LLM requests through it for filtering.
Correct answer
D. Use the guardrails parameter within the COMPLETE function's options object to enable PII, jailbreak, and prompt injection detection.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Install and configure the separate "Cortex Guard" extension in your Snowflake account.** - This option suggests installing an independent extension named "Cortex Guard." If such an extension exists, it would imply a separate component outside the built-in Cortex safety controls. However, the challenge mentions built-in Cortex Guard safety features for function calls, not an external extension. This makes option A unlikely to be the correct path for activating built-in guardrails. 2. **B. Set the account-level parameter CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED = TRUE to apply guardrails globally.** - This option proposes a global, account-wide setting. While many systems use account-level toggles for global features, the phrasing “CORTEX_GUARD_ENABLED” is plausible as a dedicated flag. However, applying guardrails globally might be too broad for per-function or per-call customization, and the scenario emphasizes enabling the built-in Cortex Guard features for function calls, which is typically controlled at a more granular level (e.g., per function or per COMPLETE invocation) rather than a blanket account-wide switch. 3. **C. Create a separate CORTEX_GUARD service and route all LLM requests through it for filtering.** - This option describes a separate service that acts as a proxy/filter before LLM requests reach Cortex, which could provide an additional layer of protection. But the challenge specifically mentions activating built-in Cortex Guard safety features for function calls, not introducing an external filtering service. This approach adds architectural complexity and may bypass or duplicate Cortex’s native safeguards rather than enabling them directly. 4. **D. Use the guardrails parameter within the COMPLETE function's options object to enable PII, jailbreak, and prompt injection detection.** - This option targets the function-level configuration, aligning with how many platforms expose safety features through parameters or options in the function call. It directly references enabling guardrails (PII, jailbreak, prompt injection) inside the COMPLETE function’s options, which is the most precise mechanism to activate the built-in Cortex Guard features for those function calls. Correct Proposition **The most suitable and efficient proposition for activating built-in Cortex Guard safety features for function calls is:** **D. Use the guardrails parameter within the COMPLETE function's options object to enable PII, jailbreak, and prompt injection detection.** Detailed Explanation 1. **Understanding Cortex Guard in Snowflake Cortex COMPLETE:** - Cortex Guard refers to built-in safety features designed to detect and mitigate harmful, biased, or unsafe content, as well as to prevent leakage of sensitive data (PII). These features are typically applied at the level of how the function is invoked, not via an external extension or global toggle. - The COMPLETE function in Cortex is a user-facing entry point for LLM interactions, so enabling safety checks here ensures that every invocation is screened according to policy. 2. **Granularity and scope of safety controls:** - Enabling guardrails at the function call level provides precise control. It allows different functions or workflows to opt in or out of specific safeguards as needed, without affecting other parts of the system. - This aligns with best practices for compliance and security, ensuring that PII protection, jailbreak detection, and prompt-injection safeguards are applied where most relevant. 3. **Command/Options structure:** - The guardrails parameter within the COMPLETE function’s options object is the correct mechanism to turn on and configure safety checks. This design keeps guardrail configuration declarative and auditable, tying policy enforcement directly to the function invocation. 4. **Why the other options are less suitable:** - A: An external extension (Cortex Guard) would introduce additional maintenance, potential drift from built-in policies, and possible gaps if not perfectly integrated with Cortex’s native guardrails. - B: An account-level toggle could lead to unintended global behavior, making it harder to enforce per-use-case compliance and auditability, and may not reflect granular policy needs. - C: A separate gate/service adds complexity and latency, and risks bypassing Cortex’s native, versioned safety policies instead of leveraging them directly. Conclusion To properly activate and configure built-in Cortex Guard safety for function calls in a Snowflake Cortex COMPLETE context, use the guardrails parameter inside the COMPLETE function’s options object to enable and customize PII, jailbreak, and prompt injection detection. This approach ensures precise, auditable, and policy-aligned protection at the point of invocation.

Question 8: Analyzing LLM Function Costs Scenario: As a Snowflake administrator, you need to create a report showing the average cost incurred per call to the COMPLETE function. Challenge: Using the ACCOUNT_USAGE views, which query correctly calculates the "average credits consumed per COMPLETE function call"?
A. SELECT credits_used / query_id FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY;
Correct answer
B. SELECT SUM(credits_used) / COUNT(query_id) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';
C. SELECT total_tokens / total_credits FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY;
D. SELECT SUM(credits_used) FROM WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY;
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. `SELECT credits_used / query_id FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY;`** - This query attempts to compute a ratio of credits used to a query identifier. However, dividing credits by a non-numeric identifier (query_id) does not yield meaningful average cost per COMPLETE function call. It also lacks filtering for the specific function name and does not aggregate across calls. 2. **B. `SELECT SUM(credits_used) / COUNT(query_id) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';`** - This query sums the total credits consumed by all usage records for the COMPLETE function and divides by the number of usage records (calls) for COMPLETE. This yields the average credits consumed per COMPLETE function call, which matches the requirement. It correctly filters to function_name = 'COMPLETE' and uses proper aggregation. 3. **C. `SELECT total_tokens / total_credits FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY;`** - This computes a ratio of total tokens to total credits over the entire table without filtering to a single function call, and without performing per-call averaging. It does not compute the average credits per COMPLETE function call, so it’s not appropriate for the stated goal. 4. **D. `SELECT SUM(credits_used) FROM WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY;`** - This query looks at warehouse credits usage, unrelated to the Cortex function usage. It does not filter for the COMPLETE function and does not perform averaging per call. It does not answer the question. ### Correct Proposition **The correct query is:** **B. `SELECT SUM(credits_used) / COUNT(query_id) FROM CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE';`** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Goal understanding:** - You want the average credits consumed per COMPLETE function call. That means you need the total credits spent on COMPLETE calls divided by the number of COMPLETE calls. 2. **Data source and relevance:** - The CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY view records usage details for function calls, including credits_used and function_name. This makes it the appropriate source for calculating per-function call costs. - The WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY view (option D) tracks warehouse credits and is not relevant to function-level usage. 3. **Aggregation logic:** - Use SUM(credits_used) to get the total credits spent on all COMPLETE function calls. - Use COUNT(query_id) (or COUNT(*) if appropriate) to get the number of COMPLETE function call records. - Divide the total credits by the number of calls to obtain the average credits per call for the COMPLETE function. 4. **Filtering:** - WHERE function_name = 'COMPLETE' is essential to restrict the calculation to the COMPLETE function only. Without this, the result would mix costs from all functions. 5. **Interpretation:** - The result yields the average credit cost per COMPLETE function call, enabling you to report or monitor the per-call cost over time. ### Conclusion Choose B for the correct, precise calculation of average credits consumed per COMPLETE function call using ACCOUNT_USAGE data.
Domain
Cost and Usage Analytics (ACCOUNT_USAGE)

Question 9: Optimizing a Multi-Function Document Pipeline Scenario: You have a query that runs CLASSIFY_TEXT, SUMMARIZE, and EXTRACT_ANSWER on every single document in a large table of 10,000 documents. This is proving to be very slow and expensive. Challenge: What is the most effective optimization to improve performance and reduce costs without losing necessary insights?
A. Increase the max_tokens parameter for the SUMMARIZE function to process documents faster.
Correct answer
B. Separate the three operations into different queries and use CLASSIFY_TEXT first to filter documents, only running the more expensive operations (SUMMARIZE, EXTRACT_ANSWER) on documents that need them.
C. Add a LENGTH filter to process fewer documents.
D. Add parallel processing hints to the query to distribute the workload.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Increase the max_tokens parameter for the SUMMARIZE function to process documents faster - Increasing max_tokens generally means the function will generate longer outputs, which increases processing time and cost per document, not speed. This is unlikely to reduce total runtime or cost when processing 10,000 documents. 2. B. Separate the three operations into different queries and use CLASSIFY_TEXT first to filter documents, only running the more expensive operations (SUMMARIZE, EXTRACT_ANSWER) on documents that need them - This approach introduces a two-stage pipeline: - Stage 1: CLASSIFY_TEXT quickly identifies the subset of documents that warrant deeper processing. - Stage 2: Only the filtered subset undergoes SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER. - Benefits: - Reduces the number of documents processed by expensive functions. - Preserves insights by ensuring only relevant documents incur higher costs. - Improves overall throughput and lowers compute usage. 3. C. Add a LENGTH filter to process fewer documents - A LENGTH filter would reduce the document pool by a simple heuristic (e.g., documents shorter than a threshold). However: - It risks excluding documents that may still require summarization or answer extraction. - It may produce incomplete insights if important items are filtered out. - While it reduces workload, it could compromise the completeness and usefulness of results. 4. D. Add parallel processing hints to the query to distribute the workload - Parallelization can improve throughput by leveraging multiple compute units. - However: - Without an effective filtering step, parallelizing all three operations on all documents still processes all work, just faster. - If costs are tied to per-document processing, parallelization may increase peak resource usage and potentially cost, unless the system handles scalability efficiently. - Parallelism helps performance, but it does not inherently reduce the number of expensive operations executed. Correct Proposition The most effective optimization is: B. Separate the three operations into different queries and use CLASSIFY_TEXT first to filter documents, only running the more expensive operations (SUMMARIZE, EXTRACT_ANSWER) on documents that need them Detailed Explanation 1. Why the pipeline order matters: - CLASSIFY_TEXT is typically cheaper and faster than SUMMARIZE and EXTRACT_ANSWER. - By classifying first, you quickly triage documents and avoid applying expensive processing to documents that don’t require it. 2. How this reduces cost and improves performance: - Reduces the total number of documents that incur high-cost operations. - Lowers compute time and resource usage per document, leading to faster overall throughput for the same or lower cost. - Maintains necessary insights by ensuring only the subset needing deeper analysis is processed further. 3. Risks and mitigations: - Ensure CLASSIFY_TEXT is reliable enough to avoid false negatives (missing documents that should be summarized). - Mitigation: set reasonable thresholds, review edge cases, or implement a downstream re-check for borderline cases. - Monitor the pipeline to adjust the filter criteria as data distribution changes over time. 4. Why the other options are less effective: - A: Increasing max_tokens for SUMMARIZE increases cost and does not reduce total processing time across all documents. - C: A LENGTH filter can exclude potentially important documents and degrade result quality. - D: Parallel processing improves throughput but does not reduce the total number of expensive operations; it may also increase peak costs if not managed carefully. Conclusion Option B provides the best balance of speed and cost reduction by limiting expensive processing to only the documents that truly need it, while preserving the necessary insights.

Knowledge Area: Performance and cost optimization in data pr

Question 10: Understanding Token-Based Billing Scenario: Your team is planning several AI projects and needs to accurately forecast costs. You will be using COMPLETE, EMBED_TEXT, and performing vector similarity searches. Challenge: Which THREE of the following statements about token consumption and costs are correct?
Correct selection
A. The COMPLETE function charges for both the tokens in the input prompt and the tokens in the generated output.
B. The TRANSLATE function charges per character in the source text, not per token.
Correct selection
C. Vector similarity functions like VECTOR_COSINE_SIMILARITY consume no Cortex credits as they are pure computation on the warehouse.
D. The CLASSIFY_TEXT function has a fixed cost per call, regardless of input text length.
Correct selection
E. The EMBED_TEXT functions charge per token in the input text being embedded.
Overall explanation
Here are the evaluations and detailed explanations for each proposition. Proposition A - Statement: The COMPLETE function charges for both the tokens in the input prompt and the tokens in the generated output. - Assessment: Likely true. - Explanation: - Many token-based LLM/payment models bill for both the input tokens (the prompt you send) and the output tokens (the model’s response). This is a common model where you pay for the total tokens processed (prompt) plus the tokens produced (completion). If COMPLETE is a function that returns a generated result based on your input, it typically incurs costs for both sides. Therefore, this proposition is correct. Proposition B - Statement: The TRANSLATE function charges per character in the source text, not per token. - Assessment: Likely false. - Explanation: - Most tokenization-based billing models charge per token, not per character. A “token” is a unit the model uses for processing (which may be roughly a word or subword, depending on the tokenizer). Billing per character would be inconsistent with standard token-based pricing and would introduce fragmentation across languages and encodings. Therefore, this proposition is incorrect. Proposition C - Statement: Vector similarity functions like VECTOR_COSINE_SIMILARITY consume no Cortex credits as they are pure computation on the warehouse. - Assessment: Potentially true but scenario-dependent; treat as likely true in this context. - Explanation: - If vector similarity computations are executed entirely within a data warehouse (or a dedicated, internal compute layer) and are not billed as token-based API calls, some platforms may categorize them as pure internal compute that does not consume Cortex credits (i.e., no external API usage tokens). If the platform design explicitly separates warehouse compute from Cortex credit usage, then such functions could incur no Cortex credits. Given the wording, this proposition is plausible and commonly aligned with “internal compute” not billed as API tokens. So, this is a reasonable true statement in this scenario, though it’s important to confirm with the specific pricing model. Proposition D - Statement: The CLASSIFY_TEXT function has a fixed cost per call, regardless of input text length. - Assessment: Likely false. - Explanation: - Most classify- or analyze-type functions are billed per input size (tokens/characters) because longer inputs require more processing. A fixed per-call cost regardless of input length would not align with proportional processing effort. Therefore, this proposition is incorrect. Proposition E - Statement: The EMBED_TEXT functions charge per token in the input text being embedded. - Assessment: True. - Explanation: - Embedding typically involves converting text into a vector representation, which is proportional to the amount of content processed. Billing per token (or per unit of input tokens) is standard for embedding services to reflect the amount of data being embedded. Therefore, this proposition is correct. Summary of correct propositions - Correct: A, C, E - A: COMPLETE charges for input tokens and output tokens. - C: VECTOR_COSINE_SIMILARITY consumes no Cortex credits (in the assumed warehouse-based execution model). - E: EMBED_TEXT charges per input token. - Incorrect: B, D - B: TRANSLATE charging per character is unlikely; typically per token. - D: CLASSIFY_TEXT having a fixed per-call cost regardless of input length is unlikely; usually scales with input size.
Domain
Knowledge area: Costing and billing (token-based pricing) fo

Question 11: Granular Cost Monitoring Scenario: You need to create a detailed report that breaks down Cortex LLM usage by user, model, and function, showing the exact number of input and output tokens consumed. Challenge: Which ACCOUNT_USAGE view provides this granular, per-function-call level of detail?
A. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY
Correct answer
B. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY
C. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.METERING_HISTORY
D. SNOWFLAKE.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.FUNCTIONS
Overall explanation
1. A. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY - What it is: A standard Snowflake view that records SQL query history, including text, start/end times, and some metrics. - Relevance to question: It provides per-query data, but not granular per-function-call level usage for Cortex LLMs. It won’t break down usage by user, model, and function or show exact input/output tokens for each function call. - Why not correct: It’s too coarse for per-function-call granularity and does not include Cortex-specific usage or token accounting. It mainly covers traditional SQL query activity. 2. B. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY - What it is: A Cortex-specific usage history view designed to expose granular per-function-call metrics for Cortex LLM usage, typically including user, model, function, and timing, and exact input/output token counts per function invocation. - Relevance to question: Directly matches the requirement to break down Cortex LLM usage by user, model, and function, showing exact numbers of input and output tokens consumed for each function call. - Why correct: It provides the granular, per-function-call detail needed to create a detailed report of Cortex LLM usage. 3. C. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.METERING_HISTORY - What it is: A view that tracks metering data for Snowflake usage, potentially including compute and resource consumption across warehouses, virtual warehouses, and other operations. - Relevance to question: Could be useful for overall cost and usage trends but is not Cortex-specific and does not provide per-function-call token-level detail by user/model/function. - Why not correct: It lacks the Cortex-function granularity and per-call token accounting required by the scenario. 4. D. SNOWFLAKE.INFORMATION_SCHEMA.FUNCTIONS - What it is: A catalog view that lists user-defined and system functions, along with metadata such as argument types and return types. - Relevance to question: Useful for discovering functions and their signatures, not for usage analytics or token-level metering. - Why not correct: It does not contain usage data, token counts, or per-user/model/function call history. Correct Proposition - The most suitable choice is: B. SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY Detailed Explanation 1. Granular requirement alignment: - The scenario asks for granular, per-function-call level detail of Cortex LLM usage, including exact input and output token counts, broken down by user, model, and function. This is precisely what a Cortex-specific usage history view is designed to deliver. 2. What the Cortex_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY view provides: - Per-call granularity: Records each invocation of a Cortex function, enabling exact token accounting for both inputs and outputs. - User attribution: Associates usage with the calling user, enabling user-level reporting. - Model-level detail: Attributes usage to the specific Cortex model used (e.g., gpt-4, other LLM variants) for cost and usage analysis. - Function-level breakdown: Separates by the function invoked, essential to understand which capabilities or tools were employed during a session. - Exact token counts: Captures the precise number of input tokens consumed and the number of tokens produced in the output, enabling accurate cost and throughput calculations. 3. Why other views are not suitable: - QUERY_HISTORY: While it covers SQL-level query activity, it does not capture Cortex function calls, models, or token-level LLM usage details needed for granular cost monitoring. - METERING_HISTORY: Useful for high-level metering, but lacks Cortex-specific per-function-call granularity and token-by-token accounting. - INFORMATION_SCHEMA.FUNCTIONS: Only metadata about function definitions; no usage or token data, thus irrelevant for this reporting requirement. 4. Practical outcome: - Using CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY allows constructing a detailed report that shows, for every Cortex function invocation, who invoked it, which model was used, which function was called, and the exact tokens consumed in both directions. This enables precise per-user, per-model, per-function cost and usage analysis.

Question 12: Deriving Actionable Business Insights Scenario: You have a corpus of 500,000 product reviews and want to move beyond simple sentiment analysis to identify specific, emerging product issues and feature requests. Challenge: Which Cortex-based approach will yield the most actionable insights for your product and engineering teams?
A. Run SENTIMENT on all reviews and track the average sentiment score over time.
B. Use EMBED_TEXT to cluster reviews and then manually read a sample from each cluster.
C. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to label reviews as simply "positive" or "negative".
Correct answer
D. Use COMPLETE with a structured prompt to extract specific issues, mentioned features, and their associated sentiments, then aggregate the results to find trends.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Run SENTIMENT on all reviews and track the average sentiment score over time.** - This approach focuses only on overall sentiment trends. While useful for high-level mood tracking, it fails to identify concrete issues, feature requests, or specific product areas driving sentiment changes. It does not surface actionable items for product/engineering teams. 2. **B. Use EMBED_TEXT to cluster reviews and then manually read a sample from each cluster.** - Clustering can reveal natural groupings and topics, but it relies on manual interpretation to map clusters to concrete issues and features. This adds manual effort and potential bias, and may not consistently surface all actionable items or precise sentiments tied to specific features. 3. **C. Use CLASSIFY_TEXT to label reviews as simply "positive" or "negative".** - Binary classification provides a coarse view of overall sentiment but does not extract specific problems, feature requests, or the context needed for targeted improvements. It lacks granularity and does not enable trend analysis of particular issues or capabilities. 4. **D. Use COMPLETE with a structured prompt to extract specific issues, mentioned features, and their associated sentiments, then aggregate the results to find trends.** - This option leverages a comprehensive extraction workflow. COMPLETE can surface granular details: specific issues, features mentioned, and sentiment tied to each, enabling structured aggregation and trend analysis. It directly supports actionable insights for product/engineering teams and scales across a large corpus. Correct Proposition **D. Use COMPLETE with a structured prompt to extract specific issues, mentioned features, and their associated sentiments, then aggregate the results to find trends.** Detailed Explanation 1. **What COMPLETE offers here:** - COMPLETE allows you to generate structured outputs from unstructured text by using a well-designed prompt that asks the model to identify: - Specific issues mentioned (e.g., bugs, pain points, reliability concerns) - Features referenced (e.g., UI changes, performance improvements, new capabilities) - Sentiment associated with each issue/feature (e.g., positive, negative, neutral, with intensity) - This yields a structured dataset rather than free-form text, making downstream analysis straightforward. 2. **Why it beats SENTIMENT-only (A):** - SENTIMENT alone gives a single score per review or aggregate trend, missing the concrete actions needed by teams. Dives into root causes and feature-specific feedback, enabling prioritization and roadmapping. 3. **Why it beat EMBED_TEXT clustering (B):** - Clustering helps discover topics but still requires manual interpretation and lacks guaranteed alignment between clusters and actionable items. COMPLETE provides explicit extraction of issues and features with sentiments, reducing manual toil and increasing consistency. 4. **Why it beats CLASSIFY_TEXT (C):** - Binary labeling ignores granularity. COMPLETE can deliver multi-label outputs (multiple issues/features per review) and nuanced sentiment per item, essential for identifying which features are problematic or desired. 5. **Scalability and governance benefits:** - With 500,000 reviews, automated structured extraction enables scalable insights. Aggregation across reviews allows you to quantify issue frequencies, feature requests, and sentiment trends over time, supporting data-driven product and engineering decisions. 6. **Actionable workflow outline:** - Step 1: Run COMPLETE with a structured prompt to extract: (a) issue category, (b) mentioned features, (c) sentiment score/value for each item. - Step 2: Normalize feature names (canonicalize variants) and map issues to a feature/bug taxonomy. - Step 3: Aggregate results by time window, product area, and severity proxy (sentiment intensity). - Step 4: Identify top recurring issues and most-requested features to inform backlog, defect triage, and roadmap planning. Conclusion For deriving actionable, scalable insights from a large corpus of reviews, D is the most effective approach. It provides granular, structured outputs that enable accurate trend analysis and actionable prioritization for product and engineering teams.

Knowledge area: Text analytics and insight extraction (NLP)

Question 13: Auditing Costs for an SPCS Application Scenario: You are auditing the total cost of a Snowpark Container Services (SPCS) application that hosts a custom service which, in turn, makes calls to SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE. Challenge: Which THREE ACCOUNT_USAGE views are required to get a complete picture of all costs associated with this application?
Correct selection
A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY (for the SPCS compute pool costs)
Correct selection
B. QUERY_HISTORY (for the SQL query execution details)
Correct selection
C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY (for the serverless Cortex call costs)
D. STAGE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY (for the container image storage costs)
E. CORTEX_SEARCH_DAILY_USAGE_HISTORY (for semantic search service costs)
Overall explanation
Correct propositions: A, B, and C Detailed explanations for each proposition A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY (for the SPCS compute pool costs) - Why this is relevant: - Snowpark Container Services (SPCS) runs on compute warehouses. To audit total cost, you must account for the compute time, credits consumed, and usage by the SPCS compute pool. - WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY provides per-warehouse metering data, including start/stop times, credits used, and warehouse size. This directly maps to the compute cost portion of the application. - What you’ll learn from it: - Total warehouse usage for the SPCS compute pool involved in hosting the custom service. - Cost implications tied to that usage, enabling an accurate compute-cost total. B. QUERY_HISTORY (for the SQL query execution details) - Why this is relevant: - The custom service inside your application makes SQL queries via Snowflake. To capture the cost implications of query processing (e.g., bytes processed, query duration, and resource consumption), you need visibility into query activity. - QUERY_HISTORY documents executed statements, their durations, and resource usage characteristics across the account. - What you’ll learn from it: - Which queries ran as part of the application, their performance characteristics, and associated resource usage. This helps quantify the compute and I/O costs tied to those queries. - Note: - While WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY gives high-level compute usage, QUERY_HISTORY helps tie that usage to specific SQL activity, which is helpful for audit-level visibility. C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY (for the serverless Cortex call costs) - Why this is relevant: - The application calls Snowflake Cortex functions, specifically SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE. The Cortex usage history captures the usage and costs associated with Cortex function invocations. - If Cortex usage incurs separate charges (e.g., per invocation, duration, or other Cortex-related metering), you must include this view to obtain a complete cost picture. - What you’ll learn from it: - The volume and cost of Cortex function invocations made by the application, enabling proper attribution of Cortex-related spend. - Important distinction: - E. CORTEX_SEARCH_DAILY_USAGE_HISTORY is not required here because the scenario centers on SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE invocations, i.e., Cortex function usage, not a separate semantic search service. If the app did use a separate Cortex search service, E might be relevant, but for SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE specifically, C is the correct Cortex view. Why the other options are not part of the three required views D. STAGE_STORAGE_USAGE_HISTORY (for the container image storage costs) - Why it’s not included: - If the audit scope is to capture all costs associated with the application’s operations (compute, queries, Cortex invocations), stage storage can be relevant for container artifacts, but it’s not always required for a complete picture of the application’s operational costs, especially if container images are maintained outside the account’s stage usage. - In many Snowflake audits focused on runtime costs (compute and Cortex usage) rather than storage of artifacts, D is not essential to the “complete cost picture” of the application’s runtime behavior. - However, include only if you must account for container-image storage costs explicitly. E. CORTEX_SEARCH_DAILY_USAGE_HISTORY (for semantic search service costs) - Why it’s not included: - This view is specific to a semantic search service usage and costs. The scenario centers on Cortex.COMPLETE invocations, not on a separate semantic search workload. Including this would overstate scope unless your deployment actually uses the semantic search service in addition to Cortex function invocations. - If your architecture indeed uses Cortex Search as a separate service, you would consider it; otherwise, it’s not needed for the stated scenario. Summary - The three most relevant ACCOUNT_USAGE views to get a complete picture of all costs for this SPCS-based application that calls SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.COMPLETE are: A. WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY, B. QUERY_HISTORY, and C. CORTEX_FUNCTIONS_USAGE_HISTORY. These capture compute, SQL query processing, and Cortex invocation costs, respectively.

Question 14: Governing Cortex Search Service Creation Scenario: To prevent the unauthorized indexing of sensitive tables, your security team needs to restrict which roles can create Cortex Search services. Challenge: What is the most appropriate and precise privilege control to implement?
A. Apply masking policies to the sensitive columns to prevent data exposure.
B. Revoke the CREATE SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.
C. Configure the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter.
Correct answer
D. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.
Overall explanation
Here’s a detailed, item-by-item analysis of each proposition and the correct choice. 1. A. Apply masking policies to the sensitive columns to prevent data exposure - What it does: Masks or hides sensitive data in query results to limit exposure. - Relevance to service creation: Does not control who can create Cortex Search services. It focuses on data access within queries, not on provisioning services. - Assessment: Not appropriate for restricting who can create Cortex Search services. It addresses data exposure, not service administration or creation privileges. 2. B. Revoke the CREATE SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles - What it does: Attempts to revoke a privilege named CREATE SERVICE at the schema level. - Relevance to Cortex Search: The term “CREATE SERVICE” is not the specific privilege used to create Cortex Search services. Cortex-related objects typically require a privilege like “CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE” (or similarly scoped privilege) rather than a generic CREATE SERVICE. - Assessment: Incorrect privilege scope and likely invalid syntax for Cortex Search. Does not precisely target Cortex Search service creation. 3. C. Configure the CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST account parameter - What it does: Adjusts an account parameter that likely controls which Cortex models are allowed. - Relevance to service creation: This affects which models can be used or indexed, not who can create Cortex Search services. - Assessment: Does not implement access control over the creation of Cortex Search services. Inadequate for preventing unauthorized creation. 4. D. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles - What it does: Removes the specific privilege that allows creating Cortex Search services from roles that should not have it. - Relevance to Cortex Search: Directly targets the ability to create Cortex Search services by revoking the exact privilege required for creation. - Assessment: Correct approach to precise privilege control. Ensures only authorized roles can provision Cortex Search services while preventing others from doing so. Conclusion - The most appropriate and precise privilege control is: D. Revoke the CREATE CORTEX SEARCH SERVICE privilege on the schema from unauthorized roles.

Question 15: Efficient Embedding Strategy Scenario: You are building a RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) system. As a first step, you need to create vector embeddings for a large document library. Challenge: What is the most cost-effective strategy for generating and storing these embeddings?
A. Call SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.EMBED_TEXT on the full document text every time a user submits a query.
Correct answer
B. Pre-compute the embeddings once, store them in a VECTOR column in a table, and reference this table at query time.
C. Use the COMPLETE function to first summarize each document, then embed the summary to save tokens.
D. Use a larger embedding model (e.g., EMBED_TEXT_1024) for all documents to ensure higher quality.
Overall explanation
Proposition analysis 1. **A. Call SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.EMBED_TEXT on the full document text every time a user submits a query.** - Pros: Always uses up-to-date embeddings from the full text for each query. - Cons: Very high compute and cost, especially for large document libraries and frequent queries; repeated embedding of the same documents is wasteful; latency increases because embeddings are generated on demand rather than being reused. - Practical impact: Not cost-effective at scale. Even if you get accurate representations, the per-query cost dominates as the library grows and query traffic increases. 2. **B. Pre-compute the embeddings once, store them in a VECTOR column in a table, and reference this table at query time.** - Pros: Minimal per-query compute; embedding generation happens once during an offline pre-processing step; fast retrieval and similarity search during query time; scalable as the document set grows. - Cons: Requires upfront processing and storage of embeddings; needs a pipeline to keep embeddings in sync with document updates (inserts/edits/deletes). If documents change, embeddings must be recomputed; adds some maintenance overhead. - Practical impact: The standard, cost-effective approach for large-scale RAG systems. Balances cost, latency, and accuracy by leveraging pre-computed, reusable embeddings. 3. **C. Use the COMPLETE function to first summarize each document, then embed the summary to save tokens.** - Pros: Potentially reduces token counts for embedding inputs; may speed up processing if summaries are short and representative. - Cons: Summarization can degrade retrieval quality if essential information is lost; embeddings of summaries may be less faithful to the original content, hurting answer quality for detailed queries; adds an extra processing step and potential mismatch between query intent and summarized content. Practical impact: Could save some tokens and cost, but often at the expense of retrieval quality. Not the most reliable cost-effective strategy for general RAG needs. 4. **D. Use a larger embedding model (e.g., EMBED_TEXT_1024) for all documents to ensure higher quality.** - Pros: Higher-quality embeddings may improve retrieval accuracy and ranking of relevant documents. - Cons: Significantly higher compute and potential cost per embedding; storage impact may be modestly higher; does not directly reduce per-query cost since embeddings must still be computed or retrieved. Practical impact: Improves quality but at a higher ongoing cost. Not the most cost-effective baseline strategy for initial deployment or large libraries. ### Correct Proposition **B. Pre-compute the embeddings once, store them in a VECTOR column in a table, and reference this table at query time.** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Cost and latency considerations:** - On-demand embedding (A) incurs repeated compute for every user query, which scales poorly with library size and query volume. - Pre-computing embeddings (B) amortizes the embedding cost across all queries, yielding low latency during user queries because you only need a fast similarity search against stored vectors. 2. **Data freshness and maintenance:** - With pre-computed embeddings, you must maintain synchronization between documents and their embeddings. Any update, insertion, or deletion requires updating the corresponding vector(s). This is a manageable maintenance task and is typically automated in a data pipeline. - The other options introduce either extra processing time per query (A, C) or higher upfront/model-cost without addressing query-time efficiency (D). 3. **Quality vs. cost trade-offs:** - While C can save tokens, it risks losing critical information during summarization, potentially reducing retrieval accuracy for detailed queries. - D improves embedding quality but increases costs across all documents, making it less cost-effective at scale. - B provides a strong baseline: acceptable embedding quality with predictable, scalable costs and fast inference. 4. **Practical deployment considerations:** - Implement a pipeline that computes embeddings offline, stores them in a VECTOR column (or equivalent), and uses fast vector search during runtime. - Include a change-detection mechanism to re-embed documents when content changes, ensuring consistency between the index and the source data. 5. **Conclusion:** - For a cost-effective, scalable RAG system, pre-computing embeddings and storing them for fast retrieval is the optimal approach. It minimizes per-query costs, supports large libraries, and maintains acceptable retrieval quality with reasonable maintenance.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Machine Learning — Vector embeddings for RAG

Question 16: The Impact of Temperature Scenario: You are using the COMPLETE function for two different tasks: 1) Extracting structured data from invoices (needs consistency), and 2) Brainstorming creative marketing slogans (needs diversity). Challenge: How should you configure the temperature parameter for each task to achieve optimal results?
A. Use a high temperature (e.g., 0.9) for both tasks for maximum creativity.
Correct answer
B. Use a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for data extraction and a high temperature (e.g., 0.8) for slogan generation.
C. Use a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for both tasks to ensure reliable outputs.
D. Leave the temperature at its default setting for both tasks.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Use a high temperature (e.g., 0.9) for both tasks for maximum creativity.** - This is inappropriate for the data extraction task because high temperature increases randomness and may produce inconsistent, non-deterministic results. While it can help with brainstorming, it harms consistency needed for structured invoice data. 2. **B. Use a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for data extraction and a high temperature (e.g., 0.8) for slogan generation.** - This aligns with task requirements: low temperature for reliable, repeatable extraction of structured data; high temperature for diverse, creative slogan ideas. It targets both stability and creativity appropriately. 3. **C. Use a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for both tasks to ensure reliable outputs.** - This prioritizes consistency but sacrifices creativity for slogans. Slogans benefit from variation and novelty, which a very low temperature reduces. 4. **D. Leave the temperature at its default setting for both tasks.** - Defaults may not be optimal for each task. In many systems, default balances creativity and reliability but does not tailor to the distinct needs of data extraction (consistency) and marketing brainstorming (diversity). Requires manual override for best results. ### Correct Proposition **The most suitable approach is:** **B. Use a low temperature (e.g., 0.1) for data extraction and a high temperature (e.g., 0.8) for slogan generation.** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Task Nature and Temperature Impact:** - Temperature controls randomness. Lower temperatures produce more deterministic, repeatable outputs, which is essential for structured data extraction where exact fields, patterns, and consistency matter. - Higher temperatures increase randomness and novelty, which is beneficial for brainstorming and generating diverse, catchy marketing slogans but can degrade accuracy and consistency. 2. **Data Extraction (Invoices) Needs:** - The COMPLETE function for invoices requires consistent formatting, exact field extraction (dates, amounts, line items, vendors), and reproducible results across runs. - A low temperature (around 0.1) minimizes varied wording and extraneous results, helping to maintain stable templates and structured outputs. 3. **Slogan Generation (Marketing) Needs:** - Creative slogans benefit from a wider exploration of language, phrasing, and ideas. A higher temperature (around 0.8) encourages variety, novelty, and engaging options, increasing the chance of standout phrases. 4. **Risk of Incorrect Temperature Choices:** - High temperature for data extraction would likely produce hallucinated fields, inconsistent tokenization, and mislabeling, undermining consistency. - Low temperature for slogans would yield repetitive, dull ideas with limited diversity, reducing marketing impact. 5. **Implementation Considerations:** - If the platform supports task-specific settings, configure per-task temperatures as described. - If you must choose a single temperature for a mixed workflow, you’d compromise, but for optimal results here, separate settings per task are preferred. ### Conclusion Set the temperature to 0.1 for the data extraction task to ensure reliability and consistency, and set it to 0.8 for slogan generation to maximize creativity and variety.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Prompt engineering — Temperature control.

Question 17: Caching for Cost Reduction Scenario: Your application uses COMPLETE to generate responses to frequently asked questions. You notice that many users ask identical or very similar questions. Challenge: What is a simple yet effective server-side pattern to significantly reduce the cost and latency of these repeated calls?
A. Instruct users to use the search function before asking a question.
Correct answer
B. Implement a caching layer that stores prompt-response pairs; check the cache before making a new COMPLETE call.
C. Use a smaller, less capable model for all queries to reduce the cost per call.
D. Batch all user queries and process them once per hour.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Instruct users to use the search function before asking a question.** - Pros: Reduces duplicate questions at the user level; can weed out obvious repeats. - Cons: Relies on user behavior and discipline; not reliable or scalable as a server-side pattern; adds friction for users; does not guarantee a response without a new COMPLETE call if the exact answer isn’t cached. - Practical impact: Minimal direct impact on server-side cost/latency; still requires the actual model call for many questions unless a separate internal cache is used. 2. **B. Implement a caching layer that stores prompt-response pairs; check the cache before making a new COMPLETE call.** - Pros: - Directly reduces repeated work by serving identical (or highly similar) queries from fast cache. - Lowers cost by avoiding unnecessary COMPLETE invocations. - Decreases latency for repeated requests; cache hits are typically much faster than API calls. - Flexible: can cache by exact prompt, or by prompt + parameters (temperature, model, etc.) and by response, enabling reuse across sessions. - Cons: - Need cache invalidation/expiration strategy to handle evolving information or time-sensitive answers. - Requires careful keying (to handle near-duplicates) and serialization; may require dedup logic for similar but not identical prompts. - Practical impact: Very effective server-side pattern for cost/latency reduction with moderate implementation complexity. 3. **C. Use a smaller, less capable model for all queries to reduce the cost per call.** - Pros: - Lower per-request cost if the smaller model is significantly cheaper. - Cons: - Likely degrades answer quality, accuracy, and usefulness; user satisfaction may drop. - Not a one-size-fits-all solution; some queries require higher capabilities. - May not yield the best overall cost savings if volume is high and users compensate by asking more questions due to poorer results. - Practical impact: Risky trade-off; not a robust server-side pattern for cost reduction without compromising quality. 4. **D. Batch all user queries and process them once per hour.** - Pros: - Can reduce per-request overhead and leverage throughput savings if the system is able to tolerate latency and still meet user expectations. - Cons: - Introduces high latency for end users; not acceptable for real-time Q&A. - Complexity in handling asynchronous results and user-visible delays. - Irregular batching could still incur multiple invocations; not guaranteed to save cost if many queries are time-sensitive. - Practical impact: Generally inappropriate for interactive Q&A scenarios; not a good fit for immediate response patterns. ### Correct Proposition **The most effective server-side pattern is:** **B. Implement a caching layer that stores prompt-response pairs; check the cache before making a new COMPLETE call** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **What caching achieves:** - Reduces repeated compute by reusing prior results for identical prompts, lowering both latency and cost when the same question is asked again. - Converts many potential paid model calls into fast, inexpensive cache reads. 2. **Cache design considerations:** - Keying: Use exact prompt text plus relevant parameters (model, temperature, top_p, max_tokens) as the cache key to ensure correctness. - Expiration: Apply TTL or cache invalidation for time-sensitive content; consider staleness policies for FAQs that change slowly. - Granularity: Decide whether to cache exact prompts, normalized prompts, or include session context. Consider per-user or per-domain caching if helpful. - Cache location: In-memory cache for speed (e.g., Redis, Memcached) with a fallback to the model when cache miss occurs. 3. **Handling near-duplicates:** - Implement the option to normalize common rephrasings (e.g., synonyms, trimming punctuation) or use a hashing scheme that captures semantic similarity for similar questions, though semantic caching adds complexity. - Optionally implement a shallow rerank or small local model to map near-duplicates to cached keys. 4. **Operational concerns:** - Cache consistency: For dynamic content, ensure a strategy to invalidate related prompts when the underlying knowledge changes. - Observability: Instrument cache hit rate, latency, and costs to quantify benefits. - Security/privacy: Ensure sensitive prompts/responses are not cached where they shouldn’t be; apply appropriate eviction policies and data handling. 5. **Why not the other options as primary patterns:** - A relies on user behavior, not a scalable server-side mechanism. - C trades quality for cost without guaranteeing acceptable results. - D introduces unacceptable latency for real-time use cases and is hard to justify cost savings. ### Conclusion Implementing a caching layer for prompt-response pairs and serving from cache when possible is the strongest, scalable server-side pattern to significantly reduce both cost and latency for repeated questions.
Domain
Knowledge area: Cost and performance optimization via server

Question 18: Choosing the Right Model Scenario: Your team is evaluating different models for a new project. The tasks range from simple text classification to complex reasoning and code generation. Challenge: What is a key cost and performance consideration when selecting a model?
A. Always use the largest available model (e.g., llama3.1-405b) for all tasks to ensure the highest quality.
Correct answer
B. Match the model size to the task complexity; use smaller models (e.g., mistral-7b) for well-defined tasks and reserve larger models for complex reasoning.
C. All models have the same per-token cost, so model selection is purely a quality decision.
D. Use the newest model version available, regardless of its size or intended purpose.
Overall explanation
Explanation of each proposition for Question 18: Choosing the Right Model Challenge: What is a key cost and performance consideration when selecting a model? A. Always use the largest available model (e.g., llama3.1-405b) for all tasks to ensure the highest quality. - Why this is not correct: Larger models generally offer better quality on many tasks, but they come with higher costs (per-token compute, latency, energy use, and potentially cheaper throughput for smaller tasks). Using the largest model for every task can lead to unnecessary cost, longer turnaround times, and reduced throughput, especially for simple or well-defined tasks where a smaller model can perform nearly as well. B. Match the model size to the task complexity; use smaller models (e.g., mistral-7b) for well-defined tasks and reserve larger models for complex reasoning. - Why this is correct: This reflects the key cost and performance consideration. Different tasks have varying requirements for reasoning, multi-step planning, or factual accuracy. Smaller models can be sufficient for straightforward classification or rule-based tasks, offering lower latency and cost. Larger models can be reserved for tasks that truly require deeper reasoning, long-context understanding, or higher-quality generation. This approach balances cost, latency, and performance. C. All models have the same per-token cost, so model selection is purely a quality decision. - Why this is not correct: In reality, per-token costs vary significantly by model size and architecture. Larger models typically incur higher compute costs per token and longer inference times. Therefore, cost is a practical consideration alongside quality, throughput, and latency. Treating cost as identical across models ignores real-world pricing and resource usage. D. Use the newest model version available, regardless of its size or intended purpose. - Why this is not correct: Newest models may have improvements, but they can also have higher costs, longer latency, or behavior changes that don’t suit a given task. Without considering whether the model size, capabilities, and safety features align with the task, simply choosing the newest model can lead to inefficiency or worse outcomes. Model selection should be aligned with the task requirements and resource constraints, not just novelty. Correct proposition: B. Match the model size to the task complexity; use smaller models (e.g., mistral-7b) for well-defined tasks and reserve larger models for complex reasoning. Detailed reasoning for all points: - Cost and performance trade-offs: - Inference cost grows with model size due to more parameters and heavier computation. - Latency also tends to increase with model size, affecting user experience and throughput. - For simple tasks (e.g., basic sentiment, keyword extraction), smaller models often achieve sufficient accuracy with much lower cost and faster responses. - Task suitability: - Well-defined, deterministic tasks with clear rules may not benefit much from large models. - Complex reasoning, multi-step planning, long-context tasks, or when higher factual accuracy is required may justify using larger models. - Throughput and scaling considerations: - In production, many requests may need to be served concurrently. Smaller models enable higher concurrent throughput at lower cost. - Larger models may be used selectively in a tiered or on-demand fashion to meet peak demand or special tasks. - Practical deployment guidance: - Start with a baseline using a smaller model to establish performance and cost benchmarks. - Evaluate increments in quality against additional cost, latency, and deployment constraints. - Reserve larger, more capable models for tasks that cannot be handled adequately by smaller models. Conclusion: The most balanced and cost-conscious approach is to align model size with task complexity, leveraging smaller models for straightforward tasks and reserving larger models for tasks that truly require greater reasoning and capabilities.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Model Selection and Evaluation.
