Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Secrets Management (c
Question 110
Skipped
A team experiences latency because their required Cortex LLM is not available in their primary Snowflake region but is in a neighboring one. Which setting should be enabled, and what is the primary trade-off?
Correct answer
A) Enable MODEL_REPLICATION to copy the model; trade-off is storage cost.
B) Enable CORTEX_ENABLED_CROSS_REGION; trade-off is increased latency.
C) Configure CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST to include the cross-region endpoint; trade-off is configuration complexity.
D) Set up External Functions to route requests; trade-off is maintenance overhead.
Overall explanation
Option analysis for the Cortex cross-region latency scenario 1. A. Enable MODEL_REPLICATION to copy the model; trade-off is storage cost. - What this does: Enables replication of the Cortex LLM model to the primary Snowflake region so the model is available locally rather than fetched across region boundaries. - Why it’s suitable: Since the required model isn’t in the primary region, replicating it locally eliminates cross-region fetches, reducing latency and improving responsiveness for user queries. - Trade-off: Increases storage usage because you now maintain an additional copy of the model in the primary region. Depending on model size, this could be significant. - Practical considerations: Ensure you have budgeted storage for the replicated model and that replication can be kept in sync with updates. Consider the frequency of model updates and whether replication can be scheduled to minimize impact. 2. B. Enable CORTEX_ENABLED_CROSS_REGION; trade-off is increased latency. - What this does: Enables cross-region access to Cortex models without local replication, allowing requests to be served from the neighboring region where the model resides. - Why it’s less suitable here: The goal is to reduce latency by bringing the model closer. Cross-region access typically introduces additional network hops and potential inter-region latency, which is counterproductive when latency is the primary concern. - Trade-off: Increased latency compared to having a local replica, and potential variability due to inter-region routing, SLAs, and cross-region bandwidth. - Practical considerations: This option is useful if replication isn’t possible due to cost, governance, or update constraints, but it won’t meet the “lower latency by having local copy” objective. 3. C. Configure CORTEX_MODELS_ALLOWLIST to include the cross-region endpoint; trade-off is configuration complexity. - What this does: Allows listing the cross-region endpoint in an allowlist so that your environment can route requests to that endpoint. - Why it’s less about latency: It’s more about enabling access to a cross-region resource rather than reducing the network distance of the request. - Trade-off: Increases configuration complexity and maintenance (keeping the allowlist updated as endpoints change, auditing access, ensuring security policies, etc.). - Practical considerations: Helps when you must explicitly permit cross-region endpoints for security/compliance, but doesn’t directly address latency reduction. 4. D. Set up External Functions to route requests; trade-off is maintenance overhead. - What this does: Uses Snowflake External Functions to route queries to an external Cortex endpoint (potentially in another region). - Why it’s less ideal for latency optimization: Routing logic adds an additional hop and can introduce extra latency and failure points; it’s more about ubiquity of access rather than minimizing region-crossing time. - Trade-off: Higher maintenance, monitoring, and reliability overhead due to external service integration. - Practical considerations: Might be useful if only certain model endpoints are needed or if you need very specific routing policies, but not optimal for latency minimization when a local copy is feasible. Correct proposition and reasoning Correct answer: A) Enable MODEL_REPLICATION to copy the model; trade-off is storage cost. Detailed explanation - Objective: The team experiences latency because the Cortex LLM isn’t available in the primary Snowflake region. Replicating the model into the primary region provides a locally hosted instance, removing the need to cross region boundaries for every request. - Why A is best: Replication directly addresses latency by bringing the model closer to the user, which typically yields the most consistent and lowest latency compared to routing cross-region or external function calls. - Why B, C, and D are less suitable here: - B introduces cross-region access latency, which contradicts the goal of reducing latency. - C adds configuration complexity without guaranteeing latency improvements. - D adds maintenance overhead and potential extra latency due to routing external calls, with no inherent latency benefit over a local replica. Conclusion To minimize latency when the required Cortex LLM is only in a neighboring region, enable MODEL_REPLICATION to copy the model into the primary region. The primary trade-off is increased storage cost to maintain the replicated model.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Snowflake Cortex – LLM deployment and cross-
Question 111
Skipped
The training process for a Document AI model with 50 documents (500 pages) is taking over 90 minutes. What is the most effective way to significantly reduce training time without compromising model quality?
A) Use external stages instead of internal stages for the training documents.
B) Reduce the number of questions defined in the model.
Correct answer
C) Split the training documents into multiple smaller model builds.
D) Increase the size of the warehouse used for training.
Overall explanation
Correct proposition: C) Split the training documents into multiple smaller model builds. Option-by-option analysis 1) A. Use external stages instead of internal stages for the training documents - What it implies: Offload data staging/processing to external storage and/or pipelines rather than keeping it purely inside the training environment. - Pros: Can improve data I/O throughput and decouple data prep from training, potentially enabling better parallelism and resource utilization. - Cons: May add architectural complexity and introduce data transfer overhead if not configured properly. The time win is typically modest unless staging becomes a true bottleneck. - Conclusion: Could help in some setups, but not the most direct or reliable path to a large, consistent speedup without risking data handling issues. 2) B. Reduce the number of questions defined in the model - What it implies: Simplify the model’s task by limiting the number of questions (or intents) it has to support. - Pros: Lower model complexity can reduce training time. - Cons: Directly reduces model capability and coverage of use cases. If “without compromising model quality” is a constraint, this option likely violates that by reducing functionality. - Conclusion: Not suitable when you need to preserve model quality and usefulness; may improve speed but at unacceptable loss of capability. 3) C. Split the training documents into multiple smaller model builds - What it implies: Partition the 50 documents (500 pages) into several smaller training jobs that run in parallel, rather than one large build. - Pros: - Parallelism: Multiple builds run concurrently, significantly reducing wall-clock training time. - Resource utilization: Better use of CPUs/GPUs, I/O, and memory by spreading workload. - Quality control: If needed, you can ensemble or combine results post-build to maintain or even improve overall accuracy. - Cons/Caveats: - Requires a reliable method to combine models or to ensure consistency across builds. - Careful handling of data partitioning to avoid skew or leakage between builds. - Some platforms require merging artifacts or re-aligning tokenization/vocabulary across builds. - Conclusion: The most direct and robust approach to achieve a significant, scalable reduction in training time while preserving model quality, provided you manage integration/merging properly. 4) D. Increase the size of the warehouse used for training - What it implies: Scale up the storage/compute resources allocated to the training process (e.g., larger warehouse, faster disks, more memory). - Pros: Faster data access and computation can reduce training time. - Cons: Diminishing returns beyond a point; training time may improve but not scale linearly, and costs increase. Also, some training bottlenecks are not CPU/GPU-bound but data preprocessing or I/O or model architecture limitations. - Conclusion: Can yield speedups, but not as reliably large as parallelizing training with multiple builds. Also potential cost impact. Why C is the best fit - It targets wall-clock time directly by enabling parallel execution, which is a common and effective strategy for large-but-manageable datasets. - It preserves model quality because you’re still training on the same data; you can aggregate or ensemble results if needed to maintain performance. - It minimizes risk compared to changing the model’s scope (B) or radically increasing infrastructure (D) without addressing the fundamental bottleneck, and it has a clearer path to a substantial time reduction than A’s moderate gains. Recommended implementation hints for option C - Partition strategy: Split the 50 documents into 5–10 groups that are roughly equal in size and complexity. - Parallel execution: Run 5–10 concurrent training jobs if the platform supports parallel builds; ensure we don’t overload shared resources. - Result integration: After builds complete, validate each model’s outputs, then combine via ensembling or select the best-performing model per domain/use-case, or merge weights if the platform supports it. - Consistency checks: Maintain consistent tokenization, vocabulary, and preprocessing across all builds to avoid drift. - Monitoring: Track training time per build, resource utilization, and model quality metrics to confirm the speedup and preserve quality. Final answer - Most effective: C) Split the training documents into multiple smaller model builds.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Model Training (Document AI)
Question 112
Skipped
A Document AI model extracts data correctly from most invoices but returns inconsistent results for a specific vendor's format. What is the most effective long-term solution?
A) Process the problematic documents multiple times and average the results.
B) Use a different, larger LLM model for the extractions on those documents.
C) Increase the warehouse size used for inference queries to improve processing.
Correct answer
D) Retrain the model with additional high-quality examples of the problematic vendor's format.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Process the problematic documents multiple times and average the results - This approach attempts to reduce randomness by repeated inference and averaging. However, it does not address systematic mismatch between the vendor’s format and the model’s learned patterns. The inconsistencies are likely due to distribution shift, not stochastic noise, so averaging will not yield reliable improvements and can be inefficient. 2. B. Use a different, larger LLM model for the extractions on those documents - Upgrading to a larger model may improve performance in some cases, especially for complex or ambiguous inputs. But for a persistent format mismatch from a specific vendor, a bigger model without targeted data can still produce inconsistent results. This is a broader solution that may increase cost and latency without guaranteeing alignment to that vendor’s formatting. 3. C. Increase the warehouse size used for inference queries to improve processing - Increasing the inference “warehouse” (e.g., larger embedding/embedding-store or larger cache) affects retrieval or throughput characteristics rather than the core understanding of the vendor’s invoice format. It does not fix the fundamental misalignment between the model’s training data and that vendor’s layout. This is more about performance and scalability than accuracy on a problematic format. 4. D. Retrain the model with additional high-quality examples of the problematic vendor's format - Retraining or fine-tuning with high-quality, representative examples of the vendor’s invoices directly addresses the root cause: the model learns the specific patterns, fields, and idiosyncrasies of that vendor’s format. This improves generalization to that vendor’s documents, reduces inconsistencies, and yields a durable, long-term solution. It also scales well if more vendors with unique formats are encountered. Correct Proposition D. Retrain the model with additional high-quality examples of the problematic vendor's format. Detailed Explanation 1. Root cause and effectiveness - The issue is a distribution shift: most invoices follow common formats, but one vendor’s layout causes systematic extraction errors. Retraining with representative samples teaches the model to recognize and map that vendor’s fields accurately, reducing misreads and missing data. 2. Long-term impact - A targeted retraining strategy creates a more robust model across similar future documents from that vendor (and nearby variants). It yields improved precision/recall for the problematic format and reduces the need for post-processing corrections. 3. Practical considerations - Gather high-quality labeled examples from the vendor (correct field mappings, edge cases, variations). - Use appropriate fine-tuning or adapters, ensuring the training data covers all relevant fields (dates, totals, line items, vendor-specific fields). - Validate on a held-out set of that vendor’s invoices and monitor for regressions on other vendors. 4. Why the other options are suboptimal here - A: Averaging results won’t fix a systematic format mismatch and wastes compute. - B: A larger model may help modestly but does not guarantee alignment to the vendor’s format; it’s not the most data-efficient fix. - C: Increasing inference warehouse capacity improves throughput, not accuracy for a specific format. Conclusion Retraining (fine-tuning) the model with rich, high-quality examples of the problematic vendor’s invoices is the most effective long-term solution to achieve consistent, accurate extractions.

Question 113
Skipped
In a RAG application using Cortex Analyst, which component is fundamentally responsible for translating a natural language question like "What were our top-selling products last quarter?" into a valid SQL query?
A) The COMPLETE function, when provided with the database schema.
B) Cortex Search, which performs the text-to-SQL translation.
Correct answer
C) The Semantic Model, which provides the metadata Cortex Analyst uses.
D) The REST API, which automatically translates questions without configuration.
Overall explanation
Here is the breakdown for each proposition, followed by the conclusion. 1) A. The COMPLETE function, when provided with the database schema. - Why this might be considered: If there is a component named COMPLETE function that can take a database schema and generate SQL from natural language, someone could argue it “translates” questions to SQL. - Why it’s likely incorrect: In Cortex Analyst, there isn’t a standard component widely documented as a “COMPLETE function” responsible for NL-to-SQL translation. Even if such a function exists, the NL-to-SQL translation typically relies on a higher-level modeling layer that uses metadata, not a standalone function called COMPLETE. The translation step is usually integrated with semantic understanding rather than a single callable function from the schema alone. 2) B. Cortex Search, which performs the text-to-SQL translation. - Why this might be considered: Cortex Search is the search/semantic layer in Cortex ecosystems, and some systems reuse “search” pipelines to drive NL understanding, including translating to SQL in a RAG flow. - Why it’s incorrect: Cortex Search is primarily about indexing, retrieval, and semantic search. It is not the dedicated component responsible for translating a natural language question into a valid SQL query. Text-to-SQL translation typically belongs to a layer that understands schema context and generates executable queries, which is beyond pure search indexing and retrieval. 3) C. The Semantic Model, which provides the metadata Cortex Analyst uses. - Why this is correct: The Semantic Model supplies the metadata about the database schema (tables, columns, data types, relationships) and the domain semantics. This metadata is essential for accurately mapping a natural language question to a valid SQL query. The translation process relies on understanding what the user is asking and how that maps to the schema, which the Semantic Model provides. In Cortex Analyst, you’d configure or define the semantic model so the NL-to-SQL translation has the correct schema context to generate correct SQL. - Why this is preferable: Without a correct semantic model, the NL query may be poorly aligned with the actual database structure, leading to invalid or inefficient SQL. The Semantic Model is the foundational layer that informs and constrains the translation. 4) D. The REST API, which automatically translates questions without configuration. - Why this might be considered: A REST API could be single-pointed to expose NL-to-SQL functionality and might “automatically” translate in a simplified flow. - Why it’s incorrect: The REST API itself is an interface/protocol, not the translation engine. It may carry requests to a translation engine, but it does not inherently perform the NL-to-SQL translation. Additionally, automatic translation without configuration is unrealistic; proper translation requires schema context, model settings, and potentially prompts or templates, which are typically provided through semantic/models configuration rather than a generic API alone. Conclusion - Correct proposition: C) The Semantic Model, which provides the metadata Cortex Analyst uses. - Rationale: The Semantic Model supplies the essential schema and domain metadata that enable correct NL-to-SQL translation within Cortex Analyst. The translation layer relies on this semantic context to map natural language queries to valid SQL queries. The other options describe interfaces or components that are not the primary NL-to-SQL translator in this architecture.

Question 115
Skipped
Which TWO statements accurately describe the use of Cortex Search REST APIs?
Correct selection
A) Authentication requires valid Snowflake credentials or OAuth tokens.
B) API responses are always returned in XML format.
Correct selection
C) They allow calling Cortex Search from external applications outside Snowflake.
D) They automatically create semantic search indexes on any table.
E) They can only be called from within Snowflake stored procedures.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A. Authentication requires valid Snowflake credentials or OAuth tokens** - Likely true. REST APIs that interact with Snowflake-backed Cortex Search typically rely on Snowflake’s authentication mechanisms. This can include using valid Snowflake user credentials or OAuth tokens issued by Snowflake’s auth flow. Without valid authentication, API calls would be rejected to protect data and search indices. 2. **B. API responses are always returned in XML format** - False. Modern REST APIs in Snowflake ecosystems generally return JSON, which is the de facto standard for RESTful services. XML would be atypical and would require explicit handling if supported, but there is no common expectation that Cortex Search REST APIs return XML. 3. **C. They allow calling Cortex Search from external applications outside Snowflake** - Likely true. One of the intent of REST APIs is to enable external integrations. This would allow external applications to call Cortex Search endpoints without embedding the logic inside Snowflake, enabling broader use cases and interoperability with other systems. 4. **D. They automatically create semantic search indexes on any table** - False. Index creation is typically an explicit operation. Automatically creating semantic search indexes on any table would be risky and could lead to performance and storage issues. Users usually need to specify which tables to index and how, often via a defined workflow or API call that creates and manages the index. 5. **E. They can only be called from within Snowflake stored procedures** - False. The benefit of REST APIs is to be accessible from external clients as well. While you can call them from Snowflake via external stages or internal procedures, they are not restricted to Snowflake-only usage. External applications can call these APIs directly. ### Correct Propositions **A. Authentication requires valid Snowflake credentials or OAuth tokens** **C. They allow calling Cortex Search from external applications outside Snowflake** ### Detailed Explanation 1. **Understanding authentication (A):** - Cortex Search REST APIs are secured to ensure only authorized users can access search indices and results. Snowflake supports multiple authentication methods for REST API calls, including: - Valid Snowflake user credentials (username/password or key-pair for programmatic access). - OAuth tokens issued by Snowflake or a trusted identity provider integrated with Snowflake. - Without valid authentication, API requests will be denied with a 401/403 status, preventing unauthorized access to Cortex Search resources. 2. **External accessibility (C):** - REST APIs are designed to be consumed by external clients, not just internal Snowflake components. This enables: - External applications to perform semantic search queries against Cortex Search data. - Integration with third-party services, dashboards, or custom pipelines without needing to run inside Snowflake. - Some deployments may restrict access to internal networks or require proper tunneling or API gateways, but the capability to call Cortex Search REST APIs from outside Snowflake is a core design goal. 3. **Why B is unlikely (XML):** - JSON is the standard response format for modern REST APIs due to its lightweight structure and wide language support. XML would be more verbose and is generally avoided unless specifically required. 4. **Why D is unlikely (automatic indexing):** - Automatic index creation on any table would remove user control, potentially incur high compute and storage costs, and could lead to unintended performance implications. Index management is typically explicit, requiring user action to create and configure semantic indexes. 5. **Why E is unlikely (Snowflake-only):** - The value proposition of REST APIs is interoperability. They are intended to be invoked by external clients and services, not only from Snowflake-native code. While you can invoke them from Snowflake procedures or tasks, the capability is not restricted to Snowflake-only usage.

Question 116
Skipped
An administrator needs to generate a detailed audit trail showing which users accessed specific tables containing model training data, including the exact columns queried. Which Snowflake view provides this granular level of detail?
Correct answer
A) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY
B) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.ACCESS_HISTORY
C) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.SESSIONS
D) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.LOGIN_HISTORY
Overall explanation
Option analysis 1. A) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY - Correct. This view records every query executed on the account, including which user ran it, start/end times, and the full QUERY_TEXT. By inspecting QUERY_TEXT you can identify which specific tables were accessed and which columns were referenced, giving you granular, audit-trail level detail (down to the column level, as expressed in the SQL). This is the standard source for user-level query activity across the account. 2. B) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.ACCESS_HISTORY - Not correct for the required level of detail. ACCESS_HISTORY captures events related to object access (e.g., when access was attempted), but it does not provide the full query text or a reliable, comprehensive record of the exact columns queried. It’s useful for access events, but not the granular per-query content needed for column-level auditing. 3. C) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.SESSIONS - Not correct. SESSIONS contains session-level metadata (when sessions start/end, user, client info, etc.), but it does not include the actual SQL text or the details of which tables/columns were queried. It’s insufficient for audit trails tied to specific table access and column usage. 4. D) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.LOGIN_HISTORY - Not correct. LOGIN_HISTORY tracks login attempts and user authentication events, not the queries executed or the data access performed. It does not provide the query text or object-level access details needed for audit trails of model training data access. Correct proposition A) SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY Detailed explanation - What it contains: - USER_NAME: who ran the query. - START_TIME / END_TIME: when the query started and finished. - QUERY_TEXT: the full SQL text executed, enabling you to see exactly which tables and which columns were referenced. - DATABASE_NAME, SCHEMA_NAME, OBJECT_NAME: information about which database objects were involved, aiding attribution to specific tables (e.g., tables containing model training data). - Other execution metrics (e.g., total_elapsed_time, ROWS_PRODUCED) that help in auditing and forensics. - Why it provides granular detail: - The combination of USER_NAME, QUERY_TEXT, and object references allows you to reconstruct precisely which users accessed which tables and which columns within those tables were queried. - You can programmatically parse QUERY_TEXT to extract column-level references, subqueries, and joins, giving you a detailed audit trail aligned with governance requirements. - How to use it for audits: - Query_HISTORY can be joined with other ACCOUNT_USAGE views if needed (e.g., to map to roles or to enrich with additional context). - Regularly export or snapshot QUERY_HISTORY for compliance records. - Implement a policy or alerting rule to flag queries that reference sensitive columns or access to specific training-data tables. In summary, for a detailed audit trail showing which users accessed specific tables containing model training data and the exact columns queried, SNOWFLAKE.ACCOUNT_USAGE.QUERY_HISTORY (Option A) is the appropriate and supported source.

Question 117
Skipped
A data pipeline uses a Dynamic Table that refreshes hourly, calling the Cortex COMPLETE function for enrichment. The team observes higher-than-expected costs. Which strategy addresses the root cause of the cost (token consumption) rather than a symptom?
A) Enable result caching for the Dynamic Table.
B) Schedule the Dynamic Table to refresh less frequently.
Correct answer
C) Pre-process the input text to remove unnecessary content, reducing token count.
D) Use a smaller max_tokens value in the COMPLETE function.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. A. Enable result caching for the Dynamic Table - This could reduce repeated work by reusing previous results, but it targets repeating requests rather than the inherent token cost of each COMPLETE enrichment. If the pipeline often processes similar inputs, caching can lower overall usage, but it doesn’t address the fundamental token volume per run. 2. B. Schedule the Dynamic Table to refresh less frequently - Reducing refresh frequency lowers how often new data is ingested and enriched, which can cut total token consumption. However, this changes data freshness and may not address token use per enrichment run if the same amount of content is still processed in each call when refreshed. It mitigates symptoms (frequency) more than the root cause (per-run token size/cost). 3. C. Pre-process the input text to remove unnecessary content, reducing token count - This directly lowers the number of tokens sent to the COMPLETE function by trimming inputs to only what’s needed. It reduces both input token usage and potential output length (if shorter inputs lead to shorter outputs), addressing the root cause of excessive token consumption rather than a side effect like frequency or caching. 4. D. Use a smaller max_tokens value in the COMPLETE function - Lowering max_tokens reduces potential output tokens, which lowers cost per call. However, it constrains the result length and may degrade enrichment quality or completeness. It mitigates cost per call but doesn’t reduce input token usage, so it’s more of a constraint on output than a holistic root-cause solution. Correct Proposition The most effective strategy to address the root cause (token consumption) rather than a symptom is: C. Pre-process the input text to remove unnecessary content, reducing token count Detailed Explanation - Understanding token cost: - In Cortex COMPLETE, you are charged for tokens in the input and output. Longer inputs and longer outputs both drive higher costs. - Why pre-processing helps: - By trimming the input to only the essential content, you reduce the number of tokens sent to the enrichment step. This lowers both input tokens and, potentially, the resulting output length, yielding a lower overall cost per enrichment. - Why alternatives are less ideal as root-cause fixes: - A (caching) helps if there are many identical or similar inputs, but it doesn’t reduce token usage for unique inputs and can add cache management complexity. - B (refresh frequency) reduces total runs but does not decrease tokens per run; freshness is traded for cost, which may not be acceptable for all use cases. - D (lower max_tokens) reduces per-call cost but can degrade the quality and usefulness of the enrichment, and it also doesn’t address input token volume. - Practical considerations: - Define a minimum viable content set for enrichment to preserve value. - Consider lightweight summarization or field extraction before the COMPLETE call to strip non-essential data. - Monitor token usage per run to quantify the impact of pre-processing. Conclusion Option C is the most direct and effective approach to address the root cause of elevated token costs in this pipeline.

Question 118
Skipped
A role named DOC_AI_PROD can see a Document AI model in the schema but cannot execute the !PREDICT function on documents in a stage named PROD_STAGE. The role has USAGE on the schema and OPERATE on the warehouse. Using the provided pseudo-code, what is the most efficient way to diagnose the issue?
A) SHOW GRANTS ON WAREHOUSE inference_wh;
B) LIST @PROD_STAGE;
Correct answer
C) SELECT SYSTEM$CHECK_PRIVILEGE('DOC_AI_PROD', 'CREATE SNOWFLAKE.ML.DOCUMENT_INTELLIGENCE', 'SCHEMA', 'my_schema');
D) ALTER ROLE DOC_AI_PROD GRANT OWNERSHIP ON MODEL my_model;
Overall explanation
Option C is the most efficient. 1) A) SHOW GRANTS ON WAREHOUSE inference_wh; - Why not: This only reveals privileges on the warehouse. It may show who can use the warehouse, but it doesn’t diagnose whether the role has the necessary rights to invoke the Document AI model or the specific ML privileges required for !PREDICT. It’s useful context but not a direct diagnostic for the failure. 2) B) LIST @PROD_STAGE; - Why not: This lists the files in the stage, not the permissions or the ability to execute the prediction. If the issue is a privilege/permission problem, listing the stage won’t identify the root cause. It may help if the failure is due to missing data, but not for access/privilege debugging. 3) C) SELECT SYSTEM$CHECK_PRIVILEGE('DOC_AI_PROD', 'CREATE SNOWFLAKE.ML.DOCUMENT_INTELLIGENCE', 'SCHEMA', 'my_schema'); - Why this is best: This built-in diagnostic checks whether the role DOC_AI_PROD has the specific privilege required to create or operate the Snowflake ML document intelligence component within the target schema. Since the role can see the model but cannot execute !PREDICT, a privilege check directly targets whether the role has the necessary privilege to run the model/prediction operation. It quickly confirms or denies the exact permission needed for the operation in question. 4) D) ALTER ROLE DOC_AI_PROD GRANT OWNERSHIP ON MODEL my_model; - Why not: This changes ownership and is a broad, risky permission grant, not a diagnostic. It may fix issues only by granting broad control, but it’s not an efficient or appropriate diagnostic step and could introduce security/permissive concerns. Conclusion - The most efficient diagnostic is C: SELECT SYSTEM$CHECK_PRIVILEGE('DOC_AI_PROD', 'CREATE SNOWFLAKE.ML.DOCUMENT_INTELLIGENCE', 'SCHEMA', 'my_schema'); This directly verifies whether the role has the required privilege to work with Snowflake ML in the specified schema, aligning with the failure to execute !PREDICT.

Question 119
Skipped
An Aggregation Policy with MIN_GROUP_SIZE => 100 is applied to a customer transactions table. A user runs a query to calculate the AVG(SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.SENTIMENT(feedback_text)) grouped by product_category and customer_region. The query fails. What is the most specific reason for the failure?
A) The query does not include a HAVING COUNT(*) >= 100 clause.
Correct answer
B) One or more of the resulting groups (product_category, customer_region) contains fewer than 100 rows.
C) The CORTEX.SENTIMENT function is not allowed in queries against tables with Aggregation Policies.
D) The user's role does not have the APPLY AGGREGATION POLICY privilege.
Overall explanation
Options Analysis 1. **A) The query does not include a HAVING COUNT(*) >= 100 clause** - This is not the correct reason. The MIN_GROUP_SIZE policy enforces a minimum number of rows per group automatically; you don’t need to add a HAVING clause to satisfy the policy. The absence or presence of a HAVING clause does not determine whether the policy is violated. 2. **B) One or more of the resulting groups (product_category, customer_region) contains fewer than 100 rows** - This is the most specific and correct reason. With an Aggregation Policy enforcing MIN_GROUP_SIZE = 100, any group that has fewer than 100 rows is excluded or the query fails for those groups. Since the query groups by product_category and customer_region, any group combination that doesn’t reach 100 rows triggers the policy restriction, causing the failure. 3. **C) The CORTEX.SENTIMENT function is not allowed in queries against tables with Aggregation Policies** - This is unlikely. Aggregation Policies typically affect the aggregation result sets (group sizes, etc.) rather than prohibiting user-defined or external scalar/analytic functions. There is no standard restriction that prohibits CORTEX.SENTIMENT under an aggregation policy, assuming the function is properly defined and permissions allow its use. 4. **D) The user's role does not have the APPLY AGGREGATION POLICY privilege** - There is no common privilege named “APPLY AGGREGATION POLICY” in Snowflake. Aggregation Policy behavior is generally enforced at query execution time based on policy definition and the caller’s role privileges to access the data. The error would more likely indicate a data- or policy-based violation rather than a missing privilege of this kind. Correct Proposition **B. One or more of the resulting groups (product_category, customer_region) contains fewer than 100 rows** Detailed Explanation 1. Understanding Aggregation Policy MIN_GROUP_SIZE: - An Aggregation Policy can specify a MIN_GROUP_SIZE, which requires that every group in a grouped query has at least that many rows. If any resulting group fails to meet this minimum, the policy prevents the group from being returned (and can cause the query to fail if the policy enforces this strictly). 2. How this applies to the query: - The query groups by product_category and customer_region and computes AVG(SNOWFLAKE.CORTEX.SENTIMENT(feedback_text)). Each (product_category, customer_region) pair forms a group. If any such pair has fewer than 100 rows in the underlying data, the policy is violated for that group. 3. Why the function itself isn’t the culprit: - The CORTEX.SENTIMENT function is invoked per row (or per group for the aggregate), but the Aggregation Policy’s MIN_GROUP_SIZE acts on the group size, not on the function’s legitimacy or capability. Therefore, the failure is tied to group size rather than function usage. 4. Why other options are less specific or incorrect: - Option A is incorrect because HAVING clauses do not define or enforce the policy; the policy enforces minimum group sizes regardless of HAVING. - Option C is unlikely unless there is a documented restriction of the function under policies, which is not implied by a MIN_GROUP_SIZE violation. - Option D is not aligned with standard Snowflake privilege naming and, more importantly, the failure would indicate permission rather than policy-based group size constraints. Conclusion The query fails because one or more groups defined by (product_category, customer_region) have fewer than 100 rows, triggering the Aggregation Policy MIN_GROUP_SIZE = 100. Therefore, option B is the most specific and correct reason.
Domain
Knowledge Area: Aggregation Policies (MIN_GROUP_SIZE)
